Nevertheless, the federated adaptation is only successful in hybrid approach the art of biological continuous updating and amalgamating the already obtained user embeddings. They are commonly constructed when the information that is being used is an inhomogeneous set of users, and when exploited irresponsibly can be known to produce misguided suggestions, or even cause a performance decline because behaviour or interests of a user in various aspects are not matched. To mitigate such risks, new federated systems are based on high parameter-efficient ahead of time tuning, including adapter-based tuning and prompt-based tuning. In this specific instance, these special techniques are designed to only scale the smaller percentage of parameter model (e.g. lightweight adapters, or prompt vectors), and thus cause an enormous decrease in both the costs of communication and computation. It is specifically applicable in privacy-sensitive federated solutions whereby, it minimizes the number of information that is exchanged among client-specific devices and central servers, as well, it also renders the user information more secure on an individual basis. (Carreira-Perpiñán, 2017; Y. Cheng, 2024; Z. Deng, 2022; Dhaouadi et al., 2025; Duan & Xu, n.d.; S. Han et al., 2015; Hee et al., 2022; Heryanto et al., 2024; Jing & Wang, 2024; li et al., 2024; Marion et al., 2025; Prasad et al., 2022; M. Sharma et al., 2019; T. Sheng et al., 2018; Y. Song, 2024; Soundararajan, 2025; Y. Sun, 2024; W. Tang et al., 2020; Tatarnikova & Raskopina, 2025; Vasquez et al., 2021; Y. Wang et al., 2022, 2025; Weng et al., 2023; Yarashov, 2025; Z. Zhao et al., 2023)
As an example we can say about prompt tuning methods, which can be described as communication and effective adaptation-to-new tasks or to new conditions of users through the transmission and update of small prompt vectors rather than all the model parameters. Adapters based methods are rather integrated together with a model architecture and additional (modular) parts, which could be trained on a user or a device. The advantages of the adapters are that they have low memory footprint and can be easily updated hence making it possible to utilize with limited resources on a mobile device. The two strategies do not deny the legacy underlying foundation model any type of flexibility or localised personalisation.
More to the point the use of the massive Foundation Models in federated by recommendation systems is also a means of reconfiguring the traditional issues with the recommendation system like no data, heterogeneousness of data and cold start issue. The models lead to effective cross-task knowledge transfers that can be used to build powerful user representations that are generalisable even without local data or IID (not independently and identically distributed). This provides a greater effectiveness of recommendation and personalization on a large organization of users that had little previous history of interaction, or users that had less computational resources that was to be accessible on their devices.
Better still, we will be in a position to create good user interests and intricate user interaction designs as time goes on using the state-of-the-art slave architectural components such as specialised Transformer layers and group gating networks. It is more valuable, particularly in environments where there exists a federation of which the user behaviour can very easily be extremely multifaceted as well as stipulated. These types of architectural innovations are used in the creation of custom recommendation structures, which can be configured to suit the capabilities of specific client hardware, in order to make sure that a wide range of hardware can be used to provide high-quality recommendations. (Bhalla, 2025b; X. Cao et al., 2022; Casey et al., 2025; J. Chen & Zhang, 2024; Gong et al., 2024; H.-T. Hu, 2025; Jahani et al., 2024; Liao et al., 2023; Qiao et al., 2023; V. Tran et al., 2023; Y. The related literature in this field is very limited, since most of the solutions focus only on on-device inference or model adaptation through online learning, leaving the training to be carried out on external Cloud services. An interesting technological perspective is to exploit Federated Learning (FL), which allows multiple devices to collaboratively train a shared model in a distributed way. However, the main drawback of state-of-the-art FL algorithms is that they are not suitable for running on tiny devices. For the first time in the literature, in this paper we introduce TIFeD, a Tiny Integer-based Federated learning algorithm with Direct Feedback Alignment (DFA) entirely implemented by using an integer-only arithmetic and being specifically designed to operate on devices with limited resources in terms of memory, computation and energy. Besides the traditional full-network operating modality, in which each device of the FL setting trains the entire neural network on its own local data, we propose an innovative single-layer TIFeD implementation, which enables each device to train only a portion of the neural network model and opens the door to a new way of distributing the learning procedure across multiple devices. Neural architecture search (NAS), particularly one-shot NAS, holds great promise for automatically designing optimal personalized models tailored to such heterogeneous scenarios. However, the coexistence of both resource and statistical heterogeneity destabilizes the training of the one-shot supernet, impairs the evaluation of candidate architectures, and ultimately hinders the discovery of optimal personalized models. To address this problem, we propose a heterogeneity-aware personalized federated NAS (HAPFNAS) method. First, we leverage lightweight knowledge models to distill knowledge from clients to server-side supernet, thereby effectively mitigating the effects of heterogeneity and enhancing the training stability. Then, we build random-forest-based personalized performance predictors to enable the efficient evaluation of candidate architectures across clients. Furthermore, we develop a model-heterogeneous FL algorithm called heteroFedAvg to facilitate collaborative model training for the discovered personalized models. This paper proposes a trusting, low latency, and energy-efficient tiny wireless FL framework with blockchain (TBWFL) for IUAV systems. We develop a quantifiable model to determine the trustworthiness of IoT devices in IUAV networks. This model incorporates the time spent in communication, computation, and block production with a decay function in each round of FL at the UAVs. We formulate the TBWFL as an optimization problem that balances trustworthiness, learning speed, and energy consumption for IoT devices with diverse computing and energy capabilities. The second element of federated learning that would evolve in the future is the creation of more advanced aggregation techniques, which will be grounded on the differences between the value the respective clients provide and the quality of data they propose. Malicious or unreliable parties post updates of clients of applicable weight wilfully to the system with reliable and relevant updates that can be quite useful in strengthening and validating the system. This makes the weight of outliers or bad data have no such strong influence to the global model that will additionally enhance the soundness of a system of general recommendation. Additionally, the research on the inclusion of federated learning to other more potential innovations, specifically, the Internet of Things and edge computing, is prospective, in particular, the work of a mobile movie recommendation system. These systems provide the highest personalisation and user privacy through localised data processing in the edge device and provide low-latency through decentralising computation. This combination of technologies introduces an opportunity to create a scalable and versatile infrastructure that can meet the demanding computational capabilities of federated learning, the efficient utilization of resources, and enhancing the efficiency of a system and the quality of the model. and fault tolerance: Decentralised federated learning is a peer-to-peer communication framework that can be enhanced with regard to superior security and fault tolerance. Meanwhile, the cross-device federated learning structure will unlock the bottlenecks of calculating and transferring data that, specifically, on the basis of massive foundation models will demand the new mechanisms of resource management and offloading of tasks. These techniques play a very important role in the trade-off between cost of communication, and the cost of computation locally, which turns crucial in the instance of fine-tuning at scale of large models when prediction is required to predict applications such as movie recommender. To address these issues, we need to develop lean and mobile optimised versions of neural networks, which can successfully be implemented on portable devices, and which can attract fewer energy, and less resources. Further reduction of the model footprint and the computing needs could also be accomplished by pruning and adaptive quantisation - federated learning can be made available even on a broader range of mobile devices. It is also hectic that engineers and designers face in the implementation of the foundation models of these such systems especially the real-time performance and scalability in the industrial applications. The computation amounts to and communication efficiency maximisation in federated learning systems, in more detail of complex multi-modal models such as GPT-4o is an excellent problem, and often involves distributed strategies of computation to manage the colossal resource consumption, intrinsic in training and fine-tuning such models over time with massive compute clusters. in the event of federated learning, particularly by the recommender systems, must be done in a manner that will not jeopardize privacy of the users. This is not simply encryption procedure of the data, and aims at sophisticated mechanisms. One of the techniques studied nowadays is called an advanced privacy or secure multiparty computation, to make sure that the data of the user remain secure, not only during the training of the models, but also during inference. Homomorphic encryption and zero-knowledge overwhelming, e.g., are schemes of ensuring that there exist efficient structures of maintaining privacy without exposing the utility of aggregated updates in a model. Providing personalised federated learning methods would allow creating strong and understandable user representations and ensuring that privacy remains the priority. It is especially relevant since it views the issues in relation to sparse data and statistical difference between the various individuals on which it is hard to conclusively ascertain about the finesse of the users in the proper sense. Also, since user preferences may shift with time, there is always the necessity of models changing with of a changing data distribution. What cannot be defined as being thus deemed to fall under the category of “personality leakage” and what can be done to be held to proper measures on the categorizing of becoming less important is one of the greatest problems that remain unresolved fully, yet (sec. 4). So, it is extremely important to develop improved objective functions and optimisation of strategies with the stated goal of preserving personality during the federation of foundation models refinement. It is also vital to generate mechanisms of the adequate quantification of such trade-offs between the accuracy of personalisation, and the risk of leakage of personality, especially of multidimensional and intricate representations of a consumer. Another issue that is of the particular importance is that advanced models will extract sensitive user traits like personality off of seemingly innocent user-interactions and that is the ethical issue of the misuse of the data and bias of algorithms. To meet these considerations, it is necessary that there exist mechanisms that allow transparent auditing, personal governance such that inferred user features are treated in an irresponsible manner. Moreover, federated foundation models would have to overcome current distribution jumps to achieve an ongoing execution, as traditional risks might lack the ability to address distribution changes, as it must being able to educate all of the parameters and is delicate to variations in the user information. (Z. Guo et al., 2024; Priyadarsini & Raja, 2024; Rokade, 2024c; C. Shen et al., 2024; W.-H. Shen et al., 2024; W. Wang, 2024b; Wassan et al., 2025c; Woubie et al., 2024; X. Yang & Xing, 2024b; L. Zhang et al., 2024; X. Zhang, Zhou, et al., 2025; Q. Zhou, 2024b)
This infuses the relevance of adapting inventive and privacy-enhancing strategies to implement personalisation in federated learning environments that can be flexible in addressing the issue of a growing user preferences whilst creation of hard rules on privacy. Furthermore, the future area of research should be centred in combination of both advanced debiasing and advanced model optimisation approaches, to trade off a high quality explainability with a high computational efficiency. This also involves construction of explainable solutions to AI in the federated environment to ensure that users can get to know the logic upon which the personalisation is emanating out of – without compromising their privacy. The solution of ethical issues, including loss of privacy data and algorithm prejudice, in the instance of the federated foundation models will require new interpretability techniques.
Application of advanced  privacy methods whereby ability to apply varying amount of noise can be used to optimize on data utility loss.

These complexities can only be overcome by having a combination of technical protections, such as the use of differentiated privacy, homomorphic encryption and effective governance structures and ongoing auditing. Moreover, federated recommendation systems in the fight against new threats include aggregation techniques, which is resilient and post-training adversarial detection.
It is significant that the models are supposed to be resilient to the data poisoning and privacy threats, and ensure that, in case some individuals do obtain to upgrading the client, the final model integrity is not compromised. This multi-layered solution goes to the extent of implementing privacy-conscious aggregation technology that retains an ability to detect anomalies and layer-wise defence privacy mechanism admitting transparent examination of anomalies. The opportunity to make dynamic inferences on the parameters of the model based on the specific features and capabilities of the client devices opens up a variety of opportunities to optimize the efficiency of the learning process and also consider the heterogeneity of the customers.

Accuracy-Privacy Trade-offs in Machine Learning

Zhang, Gong, et al., 2025)","noteIndex":0},"citationItems":[{"id":1093,"uris":["http://zotero.org/users/local/M0u71wYg/items/PEZ3SCES"],"itemData":{"id":1093,"type":"article-journal","container-title":"Concurrency and Computation Practice and Experience","DOI":"10.1002/cpe.70153","title":"Privacy Preserving Federated Reinforcement Imitation Learning Framework With Robust Aggregation for Cloud‐Based Domestic Robots","author":[{"family":"Natarajan","given":"Hema Priya"}],"issued":{"date-parts":[["2025"]]}}},{"id":1090,"uris":["http://zotero.org/users/local/M0u71wYg/items/HUWP6HN6"],"itemData":{"id":1090,"type":"article-journal","DOI":"10.21203/rs.3.rs-6658077/v1","title":"Resisting Against Targeted Poisoning Attacks in Lightweight Privacy-Preserving Federated Learning","author":[{"family":"Zhang","given":"Hongliang"},{"family":"Xie","given":"Haojie"},{"family":"Lv","given":"Jiandong"}],"issued":{"date-parts":[["2025"]]}}},{"id":1107,"uris":["http://zotero.org/users/local/M0u71wYg/items/S379GYG9"],"itemData":{"id":1107,"type":"article-journal","container-title":"Concurrency and Computation Practice and Experience","DOI":"10.1002/cpe.70323","title":"<scp>APVFGL</Scp> : A Robust Vertical Federated Graph Learning Framework Against Poisoning Attacks","author":[{"family":"Zhang","given":"Sanfeng"},{"family":"Gong","given":"Zijian"},{"family":"Zhang","given":"Zhen"},{"family":"Wang","given":"Yang"}],"issued":{"date-parts":[["2025"]]}}}],"schema":"https://github.com/citation-style-language/schema/raw/master/csl-citation.json"} The most essential problem when developing the effective federated learning system in order to suggest the movies during mobile devices use is to find the right balance between privacy to the users and the model under question. Although federated learning offers the protection of privacy inherent in that the method does not store the raw data in a remote location, this does not mean that the approach is not vulnerable to newer attacks, including model inversion and data poisoning. There is the need as such to incorporate more privacy-saving techniques. However, these solutions have the propensity of introducing their own form of trade-offs such as worsened performance of the models or increased computer hardware requirements and thereby compounding the trade-offs of the accuracy and privacy even more. An example is differentiational privacy, which provides quantifiable privacy guarantees but with converse benefits of accuracy particularly in low-data and complex models. On the same note, the privacy offered with such technologies as secure multiparty computation and homomorphic encryption is excellent and yet they are computationally intensive to an extent of not being applicable to real-time mobile recommendation systems. (Natarajan, 2025b; H. Zhang, Xie, et al., 2025; S. Zhang, Gong, et al., 2025)

In the clinical AI, It is highly Essential to hide the data And secure the data by adding noise An insured that privacy is intact as as high as possible. However, because of the trade off When too much noise is added to hide the data, the inaccuracy in the recommendations increases rapidly. Sometimes the inaccuracy goes over the roof, so much so that the recommendations become useless. Now, second problem is that if the clinical data and the recommendations of the clinical data are to be displayed on mobile system, because the mobile system are computationally weaker than any other system for example Laptop or desktop, decoding of the hidden data becomes even difficult. Devices like mobile phones prove almost useless in decoding the hidden data for the clinical AI Due to their computationally weak nature. It has to be thought about the fact that any privacy mechanism we employ, especially on mobile recommendation systems along with other platforms like your hosting server etc. has to be created dynamic in nature. Which means that if a recommendation system is being recommended on mobile phone, it is different from the one that is being recommended on a hosting website. This is because the nature of threats and attacks keep changing from time to time. Similar is the case with data distribution. For example, in the field of medical science, new diseases keep coming in. New devices are launched that may have completely different hardware and computational power, so that the privacy mechanism that worked on previous versions of the device may prove null and void in its purpose when employed in this newly launched device. This situation may put us into a situation where we are fighting with old disease with old technologies and privacy mechanism, while the new more powerful disease comes up and does damage even more rapidly than any disease we previously encountered with our older privacy protection mechanisms. Then again, the problem that comes is to find a balance between protecting the data using privacy mechanism and the accuracy which the recommendation system as a consequence shows to the users in their device. The problem gets bigger and severe in more complex machine learning models like speech recognition and medical image analysis, where this balance between privacy and accuracy, if biased towards privacy, will result in even severely inaccurate recommended contents. For example, in speech recognition, if we add more noise to train the system with strong  privacy, this extra noise when sent out to work in real environment where user will speak words and sentences for the machine to listen to and interpret will result in wrong words being interpreted by the system. This will make the system useless for daily communication and usage. Similarly, if we apply a similar noisy machine learning model (i.e. A model trend on too much noisy data for  privacy protection), The final result when employee to real life medical applications may cause the model to miss critical features in X ray or CT scan or completely false positive reports. And even worse is that missing out on features in the images which point towards underlying disease may result in false negatives and the patient may be advised to not take any serious action. Federated-Learning Algorithm Based on Client Sampling and Gradient Projection for the Smart Apart from the noise accuracy trade-off in privacy, there is one more problem known as “poor gets poorer”. This means that if privacy mechanisms are not designed with proper balance and buyers and equity, the model may end up discriminating between different user groups or users data. In other words, the noise which is added to the data may be proportional to the magnitude of the data point. So if this kind of noise is propagated into the model the data set may start collecting into groups of small sub data (as in unsupervised learning) and the whole data set may end up looking like a collection of lumps of data at many different places on the graph. Here lies another problem. User who contribute rare type of data, for example some users may like very rare genre of movies. If we apply equitable noise to the data set of all the users these rare contributions may for sure will get clocked in too much of noise and the system may not learn much about these rare data set types and hence perform poorly. Because the whole point of Federated learning is to hide the raw data of the user, it is therefore almost impossible to tell whether the system is recommending content in all fairness to all users or not. Privacy and fairness cannot go hand in hand at some instances. The simple fact that if privacy mechanisms and measures are not designed with fairness in mind, they can lead to harming minority group of users. Especially though group of users who contribute rare genre of data set. To resolve this dilemma, we can deploy what is called “personalised privacy”. In this what happens is that the privacy protection is tailored to each user according to the data the user generates. This helps in keeping sensitive information of each user safe while at the same time system works efficiently and reliably from for each user. A new strategy is proposed known as “multi objective optimization” to handle fairness and privacy tag of work and create a balance between all the groups so that no group of users is left behind with useless recommendations on their devices. This strategy has a goal to find an optimal point among accuracy, privacy and fairness, all at the same time. There are also some technical challenges like a privacy technique named Poisson subsampling which can put too much load on the low end mobile phones or hardware and their CPU. This will exclude users who possess older phones and hence will collect correct data from only users that have high end specifications mobile phones and devices especially when they are new. Consequently the unfairness. This leads us to a warning named Matthew effect warning. It says that privacy if focused on while creating a privacy system without bringing into the game fairness, then the resulting model of Federated learning will reinforce or amplify already existing inequalities making the “poor even poorer”. (Badar et al., 2024b; Binns, 2017; Choudhury et al., 2024b; Chu et al., 2021b; Culnan, 2000; J. Ding et al., 2020; Du et al., 2020; Geng et al., 2024b; Hosseini et al., 2023b; Ju et al., 2023c; M. J. Khan et al., 2024; Kim et al., 2024d; Maheshwari et al., 2022; Padala et al., 2021b; C. Pan et al., 2024; Pentyala et al., 2022b; Rodríguez-Gálvez et al., 2021b; Salem et al., 2022; K. Sun, 2025c; Vijay, 2025b; J. Xu et al., 2021; Yaghini et al., 2023b; H. Yan et al., 2023b; P. Zhang & Liu, 2025; R. Zhao et al., 2024b)
Methodology
In this part, we will discuss about how we did the experiment and the methodology we used to perform the experiment to find the optimal point of trade-off between Accuracy and privacy in mobile recommendation system of movies. We will discuss about the approaches, methods tools that we utilised and procedures to investigate and research about or core research questions. The methodology that we are employing addresses and inquires accuracy-privacy-fairness, its trade-offs which is found and is quite prevalent in federated systems employed in mobile devices for recommendation of movies to the users. What we are most interested is to enquire the selection and processing of datasets to simulate various mobile environments in different types of devices. The devices are especially geared up to simulate resource-constrained real and physical devices; including the configuration of FL protocols which account for non-IID data distributions. Then we also discuss and employ privacy preservation mechanisms such as  privacy and secure aggregation to compute the metrics.
Data Preparation and Partitioning
In this part, we explain how we selected the dataset, what the dataset actually contains, and also what kind of preprocessing and data partitioning strategies we used so that we can simulate a real federated learning (FL) setting for a mobile movie recommendation system. For this work we mainly use the MovieLens-1M dataset, which contains around 1,000,209 explicit ratings, and we use it because it helps us to realistically simulate personalization and user heterogeneity. This dataset is also very commonly used in FL recommender system research because it is quite sparse (around ~95%), it has diverse user behaviour patterns, and it is closely related to the mobile streaming domain, where ratings can be treated similar to watch-history, likes, or content requests. For the preprocessing step, what we do first is we filter the data a bit. Basically, we remove users and movies that have less than 5 interactions, because this way we can reduce the sparsity of the dataset and also handle the cold-start issue to some extent, while still keeping the interactions that are actually meaningful for our experiment. After that, we convert the explicit 1–5 ratings into implicit feedback by doing binarization, where any rating that is ≥ 4 is treated as a positive interaction as 1 and the rest is treated as 0. We do this mainly because in real mobile app cases we usually don’t have full rating values all the time, but more like simple signals such as “watched”, “liked”, or “clicked”, and the missing ratings are treated like unobserved interactions. We also use the timestamp information to do a chronological train-test split (80/20), since it is more realistic for sequential recommendation where the model learns from past behaviour and then predicts future behaviour. On top of this, we apply per-user normalization to standardize the ratings, so that differences caused by users rating in different ways (like some users always rate high and some rate low) is reduced. To simulate federated learning (FL) in a realistic way across different types of mobile clients, we split the data horizontally (so, user-based partitioning). What we do is randomly assign users to K = 100 clients, and then each client only keeps the interactions that belong to the users assigned to it. This setup naturally creates a non-IID data distribution, because users behave differently (different preferences, different activity levels, and also different amounts of data, which often follows a power-law type pattern). We also make sure the client dataset sizes are not all the same (for example, roughly around 10–200 users per client) so that it better reflects the heterogeneity we usually see in real mobile settings, and even after processing there is still around ~45–80% sparsity left. With this overall setup, we can preserve privacy (since the raw user data never leaves the client), keep things scalable for edge/mobile devices, and more properly evaluate the accuracy–privacy trade-off under realistic non-IID federated learning conditions.
Model Architectures
Describes the models of machine learning implemented (matrix factorization) and their mathematical formulation and implementation decisions in order to make them amenable to mobile deployment. This entails adaptations to exceed the resource constrained environments by means of quantization and model pruning by taking into consideration efficient inference in a variety of mobile hardware. Furthermore, in this section, we'll talk on how these models can be particularly designed for recommender systems, like collaborative filtering and the matrix factorization approaches to guarantee high relevant and high performance to the recommender system in the federated learning paradigm. This also includes combination of novel architectures dedicated for the federated scenarios such as federated collaborative filtering or federated matrix factorizational decentralized training and privacy preservation. The mathematical formulations of such models will be presented which hereby underline the role that crucial models play in enabling privacy-preserving mechanisms, e.g. secure aggregation or  privacy that do not come at cost for model utility. The choices and the adaptation of such models are important for ensuring that the proposed federated learning framework is computationally feasible on mobile devices, and can capture complex user-item interactions for purposes of accurately recommending movies. These architectural decisions are done carefully under consideration of strength against dictionaries that isn't meet IID data distribution assumption and ability to maintain their performance under different communication constraint which is natural to mobile FL environments. In addition, the methodologies include the accommodation on the client side whereby individual devices can optimize global models using their own local data and this can be made to allow the personalization to better accuracy and privacy boundaries. The rationale of the multi-global model strategy that is embedded in a hierarchical framework to work with heterogeneous in device and data distributions will also be discussed, and will improve the scalability and personalization from end to end across the network. This approach makes personalized and efficient learning for diversity devices and data distributions possible where the approach means time departing from traditional FL paradigms also by the realization of multi-global model approach in hierarchical framework. This multi-global model strategy is in contrast to conventional federated learning strategies that usually maintain a single global model, in which instead of depending on different client characteristics and preferences, they have more nuanced approach to take care of the diversity of the data. This enables a more adaptive and resilient system which is useful in context where there is huge heterogeneity between users, and between different device capabilities.
Federated Learning Setup and Training
Describes the federated learning protocol (FedAvg) and the communication mechanism and training procedures and the way the clients are involved in the distributed training procedure. This section will describe the FedAvg algorithm as base algorithm and dedicated algorithms to deal with the non-IID data using multi-global algorithms in a 3-tier federated learning architecture. This hierarchical framework has attained better data privacy, computation efficiency and scalability, compared with the traditional client-server FL models. In particular, there are three layered architecture to include clients, edge layer and federated edge layer, which make efficient aggregation and personalized management of models. This architecture allows for a solid and robust approach to handle the non-IID data characteristics by aggregation across multiple layer and supports for multiple global models leading to a significant increase in the efficacy of the model and addressing the data & computation heterogeneity. As the significant coordinator the edge layer explores the metadata of the results obtained from training sessions of clients to give information on model states and requirements as well as to query the federated edge layer in case a suitable model can not be found locally. This multi-tiered approach is more granular in terms of aggregation and distribution of models and more adaptive to the fluid and varied nature of distribution of movie recommendations to the mobile medium. The fedge layer in turn is in charge of the storing and assuring of currency and representativness of these different global models with respect to various data landscape. This kind of structural orchestration allows well selected model and distribution strategy which is especially beneficial for case scenarios using different mobile clients with different data distributions and different computational capacities in movie recommendation system. This holistic solution provides for both the global awareness and individual preferences to be well represented and user privacy and efficiency to be kept. The information exchange between the clients and the server is based on well-known algorithms (e.g. FedAvg) however, the possibility to add custom aggregation strategies to adapt to a specific research need. The server is then used to aggregate these local updates so that it can be used to refine the global model and disseminate the improved version back to clients in order to conduct further training rounds until convergence takes place. This is an iterative process which is the main component of federated learning and thus only allow federated learning to train powerful models in a non-centralised manner, hence improving privacy. This distributed method of working helps ensure that security-sensitive data is not stored on the company servers but on individual devices and is aware of the strict data protection laws and user expectations of user privacy.
Differential Privacy Implementation
Describes the implementation of the addition of privacy using DP-SGD (step by step), i.e.: Gradient clipping, Noise addition and accounting for privacy budget using Renyi  Privacy Implementation In addition, the combination of other techniques including gradient sparsification and  coding will be considered for the sparse communication updates of the local models, such that only significant updates which are in the parameter space will be efficiently transmitted. This layering of defense mechanism provides a significant improvement of privacy on higher guarantees than basic DP, in terms of resiliency in the system against various kinds of inferrence attacks, and how efficient the resources being optimized. These in turn are important strategies to help balance the rigid requirements of protecting people's privacy and achieving acceptable model performance, in the case of resource constrained mobile environments. In addition, the proposed approach integrates the local  privacy mechanisms into the training pipeline directly in order to further improve the privacy protection. This is done by adding precisely calibrated noise on the client updates before transmitting them to the server, thus both individual contributions will be indistinguishable but not stop them from contributing to the learning of the model. This mechanism, mostly implemented by DP-SGD involving the use of per sample gradient clipping to restrain the sensitivity of single updates and then addition of calibrated gaussian noise from distribution scaled to the privacy budget (kept track of using renyi  privacy for the tight composition bounds) assures that the aggregated global model encapsulates these overarching patterns in the federation without, during the aggregation phase at the central server, memorizing and over fitting any private data of any given user. Gradient clipping clipping the L2-norm of the gradient of each sample that prevents any one client's update transitivity and unnecessarily impacting on the global model, and the noise injection makes the individual contributions statistically indistinguishable from each other or random perturbations and so formally guarantees of , - privacy bound the privacy leakage, no matter what the adversary's access to the model's outputs or model intermediates. This is not only useful for warding off sophisticated threats of inference such as membership inference or reconstruction attacks (where the adversaries may be able to infer user-specific preferences over movies from the gradient patterns), but it also is empirically useful for improving model generalisation, acting as a form of regularisation, as we will see when we touch on the bigger question of large-scale federated training scenarios where DP-FedAvg leads to production-ready models (immune to unintended memorisation of rare or sensitive user-item interactions).
Privacy Attack Models
Articulates the adversarial evaluative methods for testing for effectiveness of privacy protection mechanisms. This comprises simulating prominent attacks such membership inference attacks, attribute inference attacks, data reconstruction attacks, model poisoning attacks as observed specific for federated recommender systems in case of movie recommendations with non-IID data distributions. Membership inference attacks, for example, permit a server that is honest, but curious about, the fact that the behavior in this case of the server model and/or corresponding gradients are different according to the synthetic data that were used to train the models in shadow settings of happy but curious data providers and to infer some specific interactions between users and items. Attribute inference Attacks piggyback on uploaded model parameters and thereby infer sensitive user demographics or preferences, and even in a privacy-equipped federated setup find vulnerabilities. Reconstruction attacks by the server Reconstructing private ratings or interactions from the gradients. >80% accuracy in matrix factorization cases on data such as MovieLens. Reconstruction attacks from the server point to the risks of hierarchical FL architectures. Poisoning attacks, which is design as model poisoning or gradient poisoning attacks is a series of attacks where an adversary will make use of a small number of malicious clients in order to simulate the behavior of a synthetic of fake users to upload carefully crafted gradients in an abuse of the federated aggregation rounds. These adverse updates give the global model parameter updates, especially item embeddings, so as to artificially increase the visibility and ranking of some target movies in top-  recommendations, without any prior knowledge about benign clients' data distributions or interactions. For example to increase their attacks, attacker approximate benign user embeddings by using random sampling or hard mining techniques, and create a poisoned gradients to push the representation of target item in the embedding space is like popular movie by inherent popularities in recommender systems. Such attacks are particularly powerful in non-IID situations similar to movie recommendation datasets like MovieLens where it only take 1-5% of all clients to be malicious, and are able to increase hit rate and exposure of unpopular target movies by significant 20-50%, across users, with no negative effect on the overall model accuracy, and without any negative effect on the overall accuracy. These evaluations are helpful to quantify leakage of privacy and integrity using standardized measurement criteria, e.g. attack success rate (e.g. target item promotion rate (in top-k lists)), F1-scores in terms of discriminability of malicious influences, re-construction fidelity (in terms of recoverable private interactions), model utility degradation (e.g. NDCG drop). Empirical results confirm the resiliency a number of proposed defenses like DP-SGD which adds crafted perturbations by adding calibrated Gaussian noise so that are indistinguishable from normal updates, gradient sparsification which sends only parameter changes with significant magnitude to the dilution malicious parameter changes impacts, and multi-global model strategies of which diversifies the aggregation over heterogeous client clusters that reduces the efficacy of such attacks with 30-70% limited accuracy reduction. Such comprehensive evaluations are important in order to discover more about the boundaries of the security of federated recommender systems in practice, and especially, in real world scenarios, in a mobile environment where user bases are diverse and potentially adversarial.
Resource Profiling and Metrics
Includes specifics of measuring important computational resources of crucial interest to practical implementation of federated recommender systems on resource constrained mobile devices, e.g., CPU usage, memory usage, energy consumption, communication overhead and inference time. These metrics are profiled by using special tools like I-Prof for predicting computation time and energy impact of local training tasks on android devices, Trepn and Snapdragon Profiler for monitoring power consumption of computation at a whole system level, benchmarks like FedScale which uses real world device traces for computation speed (FLOPs/s), communication throughput (MB/s) and client availability patterns. In federated movie recommendation scenarios, with large item catalogs (for example using MovieLens, containing e.g. thousands of movies), the limited memory consumption is one of the main bottlenecks.128 dimensional item embedding has a huge memory footprint (10.2 KB), which makes it not feasible to fully store millions of items (>1GB) in typical smart phones with 4-8 GB total RAM, mainly requiring solutions such as elastic item embeddings or server-side storing of items, to limit the memory consumption of each client to 25-512MB. Computation is measured in terms of FLOPs or time per local epoch. 1-5 seconds per round on normal CPUs(more precisely 4x vCPU) are acceptable so that user can not see the too much waiting times. Energy profiling aim at <0.1% FL participation on a daily basis, for which a combination of limited continuous FL participation to <0.1% of cases (e.g. energy consumption) with adequate privacy guarantees (e.g. DP-SGD - Gaussian noise computation overhead) and regularization benefit are provided by e.g., FLeet (0.036% per day). These communication metrics are aimed at tracking gradient size uploads (e.g. 1-10MB/round in the case of embedding-heavy models) against mobile data budgets (< 100MB/hour) in favour of compression (e.g. sparsification). Latency, for on-device inference, have to be less than 200ms (top-K recommendations) for IoT-like devices (64MB RAM); and less than 50-200ms for cellphones (512MB RAM) so that the systems can be used in real-time. in a non-IID, These are thresholds to make 'acceptable' deployment: e.g. peak memory <20% device total < energy < 1mJ update round time <10s Who validated empirically to remain model to useful without too much churn Comprehensive profiling on heterogeneous fleets to measure trade offs e.g. participant selection, model pruning, for production-scale federated movie recommenders.
In order to assess the quality of recommendations provided by our federated learning system, we use a set of standard evaluation metrics that are commonly used in recommender systems research, and that correspond to various aspects of model quality such as ranking quality, retrieval effectiveness, prediction quality and user coverage. Key ranking metrics include Normalized Discounted Cumulative Gain (NDCG@10) which provides a higher weight to the relevant items found closer to the top of the list (which is the reward) through position-based discounting, Hit@10 or Hit Rate@10 which measures the proportion of users for whom at least one relevant item is present in the top-10 recommendations, Precision@10 which measures the ratio of top-10 items that are relevant, Recall@10 which measures the ratio of all relevant items retrieved in the top-10 recommendations (Jadon & Patil, 20
These metrics are computed on a held out test set (20% of the dataset) separated temporally from the training data by chronological splits (e.g. 80% oldest interactions for the train dataset), as is common in recommender systems testing. This approach is emulating real-world deployment in the sense that future interactions cannot leak into the prediction as well as allowing to robustly assess generalization to unseen user-item interactions which is important for federated problems with stochasticity as well as heterogeneity
Together with the next section which is Experimental Setup, the above components of the Federated Movie Recommendation System, including System Architecture for Federated Movie Recommendation, Dataset Description and Preprocessing, Federated Learning Model Design, Privacy Mechanisms Implemented and Evaluation Metrics and Resource Profiling and Metrics would in large part ensure an extensive and holistic methodology for the fostering of privacy preserving Federated recommender systems.
The System Architecture illustrates a layered client-server architecture around the notion of movie recommendations, both for the decentralized process training, which is based on the user devices, and for global models aggregated at the server able to work with large size item catalogue as MovieLens avoiding memory constraint by dedicated strategies as using elastic embeddings or also with large items at the server . Dataset Description and Preprocessing Describes the treatment of the real world and large benchmarks, such as MovieLens, e.g. sparsity, cold start problems, non-IID distributions common in user interaction data, normalization, splitting strategies, synthetic data augmentation in order to simulate heterogeneous client behaviours . The Federated Learning Model Design, in which the embedding based architectures can be embedded with local personalization, such as matrix factorization, or graph neural networks, adapted to on physical computation is stated, to overcome statistic heterogeneity -in which HL user show skewed preferences and interaction frequencies.
Privacy Mechanisms Implemented Provide integrated defenses as, e. g.  privacy through stochastic gradient descent, and gaussian noise injection of gradient sparsification reduce transmitted sensitive update and multi-global model aggregation across client clusters such as effectively defend the attribute inference attack of inferring demographics from model parameters, the reconstruction attack of recovering private ratings with over 80% accuracy, the poisoning attack of adversarial clients craft the gradient to boost target items by 20-50% in topk lists . Evaluation Metrics including the standardized components such as the hit rate , F1 tens for attack detection, the reconstruction fidelity, the model utility degradation, as well as accuracy as price privacy trade off measured with the privacy budgets ( epsilon ) and the utility drops of a model in an adversarial environment . Finally, Resource Profiling and Metrics does benchmarking of critical constraints on mobiles with programs and tools including I-Prof, Trepn, and FedScale traces addressing deadlines of critical resource constrictions including peak memory of 20% device RAM requirement (128 par 264 megabytes), energy less than 1mJ per update and 0.1% per day, communication in form of sparsified gradients, and inference latency less than 200 ms, viability in heterogeneous fleets, participant selection, and pruning tasks.
This frameworks include, in addition to the theoretical background. (e.g. FL convergence under non-IID data ), architectural designs. (e.g. horizontal vs. vertical FedRec paradigms ), practical optimisations. (e.g. adaptive aggregation for stragglers ) customised for privacy provisioning, resource limited FedRecs, comes with a direct answer to many of the challenges such as the amplifying impacts of non-IID data distributions on personalization disparities, the heterogeneity of adversarial attack scenarios (from poisoning through fake user or gradients attacks), as well as mobile deployment constraints. (e.g. system heterogeneity and high communication costs) 
The Experimental Setup section implement this methodology in terms of details in simulation environment (e.g. FedDemo or Flower for multi-device emulation), hyperparameter setting (e.g. local epochs, learning rates optimized for non-IID), baseline models for benchmarking (e.g. FedAvg, FedProx, centralized MF) and strict evaluation protocols (e.g. cross validation for IID/non-IID splits, multiple seeds for stability) clearing the path for empirical validation in various ways e.g. different attack levels, privacy budgets and device profiles . Through this operationalization rigid assessments of the trade-offs between the utility of the models and the difficulty of actual mobile deployments is possible with various federated recommendation strategies.
Experimental Setup
4.1 Overview
This chapter is an account of the whole experiment design with which we estimated the trade-offs between accuracy and privacy in federated learning particularly in their application to movie recommendation systems that are supposed to be run on mobile devices. To address three key research questions, which we formulated, our experiments will be aimed at. The first research question is RQ1 that investigates the impact of the different budgets of privacy on the quality of the recommendations, that is, whether the quality of recommendations gets impacted negatively or influenced one way or the other depending on the variation of privacy budgets. The second research question is based on RQ2 which would be concerned with the effectiveness of the privacy attacks since we primordially vary and change the settings of the privacy settings whether the privacy attacks will be maintained or the privacy attacks break when we do the varied settings of the privacy levels. And the third of the research questions that is RQ3 is the influence of data heterogeneity on the federated learning performance because in reality not all users watch the same type of movies or with preferences like in the homogeneous or IID case, the data is not homogenous, but non-uniform or non-IID data that may affect the federated learning performance greatly. It costs us much next to bring us to the experimental pipeline. It contains the data preparation, contains the non-IID data splitting and this is the way we cut the data into fragments that model real users and contains the in memory simulated federated learning, the other is the addition of the privacy and this is where we literally add the privacy operations, the third one is the privacy attack measurement, and the last one is the overall measure gathering. Other than all this, there was also companion mobile client that was developed by Flutter also there to illustrate the fact that it is not a simulation or a theoretical discussion but a real application that can be deployed and used by an actual mobile device.
4.2 Hardware and Software Environment
4.2.1 Computation Platform
These are all the experiments which are being run on a single work station on multi-core CPU. It does not imply any GPU acceleration, and even the size of the model (approximately 168K parameters) and the size of the dataset (100K interactions) are relatively small and render it possible to be trained on a computer. The entire series of the 30 experiments may require the use of 2.53 -hours wall-clock time. The full usage of the main memory can be about 4 GB RAM as it is used to execute the whole experiment, and the artefacts of the results (JSON, CSV, and PNG figures) occupy less than 500 MB of disk Space.
4.2.2 Software Stack
The python code in the server-side is fully python written code. The main depth-learning framework that is used to explain the model, run forward/back pass and the control of the gradient of DP-SGD is PyTorch. NumPy similarly is applicable in partitioning of data by Dirichlet distribution and general numeric operations. Pandas is used in the loading and aggregation of tabular data. The membership inference attack and popular evaluations tools make use of the Random Forest classifier at Scikit-learn. All figures are created and are publisable by matplotlib and Seaborn. Implementation of the optional server, which is founded on a Web service, like HTTP is done through FastAPI that will be deployed on distributed and mobile clients.

4.3 Dataset Preparation
4.3.1 Data Loading and Preprocessing
The MovieLens 100K data is imported to CSV format having four variables user id, movie id, rating, and time. The user and item identifiers are re-mapped to direct ones as integers with zero indices in the embedding layers with the index denoting the user or item identifier. Following the re-map process, the set of distinct users is 943, distinct items are 1,682 and records of the interactions in the dataset are 100 000. In order to form a binary rating in the regression based evaluation pipeline, because it is in a continuous form, the ratings are not binarized; on the binary classification variant which is adopted in some attack evaluation, the regression based evaluation pipeline is left continuous and binarized with a rating threshold of 4.0. (ratings ≥ 4.0 → 1.0; otherwise → 0.0).
4.3.2 Train–Test Split
The two blocks of 100,000 interactions are divided with 80,000 interaction training 80% and 20,000 interaction test 20&, random shuffled. It is split at the interaction level and not user level in which all users are guaranteed of having a small number of ratings on both test and training set. This enables one to make an approximation of per-user recommendation measures on the test set.
4.3.3 Non-IID Data Partitioning via Dirichlet Distribution
To simulate realistic mobile data distributions where each device holds a distinct and non-overlapping subset of user interactions, the 80,000 training interactions are partitioned among K = 100 simulated clients using a Dirichlet distribution with concentration parameter α. The partitioning proceeds as follows:
Step 1 – Group by User: Interactions are grouped by user ID, yielding up to 943 groups.
Step 2 – Sample Proportions: For each user, a probability vector p∼ Dir(α, α, …, α) with K = 100 components is drawn. Each component pk represents the fraction of that user’s interactions assigned to client k.
Step 3 – Assign Interactions: Each interaction is independently assigned to a client according to the sampled proportions. This introduces controlled heterogeneity: a small α concentrates most of a user’s data on one or two clients, while a larger α distributes it more evenly.
The resulting partition is inherently imbalanced. With  (the default), client dataset sizes range from roughly 100 to 1,500 interactions, with a mean of 800. This imbalance is representative of production mobile deployments where user activity levels vary widely.
Table 4: Dirichlet Concentration Parameter Configurations
α Value
Heterogeneity Level

4.4 Federated Learning Simulation Environment
4.4.1 In-Memory Simulation Architecture
The federated learning experiments are applied on the basis of the in-memory simulation that renders the in-memory variation of the Federated Averaging (FedAvg) protocol without introducing network latency and serialization expenses. In each communication round every client loads a deep copy of the current state dictionary of the global model, performs the local training on its own data partition and transmits the resulting state dictionary to the server. The server will then do weighted average (Section 3.4 of the Methodology) in order to arrive at the next global model. The design permits this complete 30-experiment suite to equally execute on a single machine and further act functionally identical to a distributed deployment.
4.4.2 Per-Round Execution Flow
Each federated learning round executes the following steps:
1. Model Distribution: The state dictionary of the model distributed around the world is copied to the 100 clients. The new Matrix Factorization module is created and obtained parameters are loaded in each client.
2. Local Training: All clients are trained with its local data component on  local epochs using SGD (learning rate 0.01, weight decay ) using a batch size of 32. The Loss of the regression variant is the Mean Squared Error (MSE) and Binary Cross-Entropy (BCE) of the binary variant. When DP is employed, graduate clipping and noise injection follows a step of DP-SGD.
3. Parameter Collection: Parameters of the current state dictionary and the local number of samples of each client are acquired.

5. Evaluation: The Held-out test set is the test set on which the global model is tested. At the key checkpoints (rounds 1, 5 and 10), a full assessment ( each with recommendation metrics ) is carried out to reduce the computational load, and only simple regression metrics (MSE, MAE) are made round-by-round.
6. Logging: Round-level measurements are recorded and sent to an in-memory logger and then written as logs on disk as either a JSON or CSV file at the end of the experiment.

4.5 Differential Privacy Integration
4.5.1 DP-SGD Implementation Details
Differential privacy is enforced at the gradient level during local client training. After each backward pass, two operations are applied sequentially:
Per-Parameter Gradient Clipping: The L2 norm of the full gradient vector (concatenated across all model parameters) is computed. If the norm exceeds the clipping threshold , all per-parameter gradients are scaled down by the factor.

This bounds the maximum contribution of any single training sample to the model update.
Gaussian Noise Injection: After clipping, independent Gaussian noise drawn from I(0, σ² · C² · I) is added to each parameter’s gradient. The noise multiplier σ is pre-computed to achieve the target privacy budget ε given the number of training steps, batch size, and dataset size.
The clipping and noise operations are applied at every gradient step within the local training loop, ensuring that each mini-batch update satisfies the local DP guarantee. RDP accountant will be in charge of tracking cumulative loss of privacy of all round.
4.5.2 Privacy Budget Calibration
The noise multiplier σ for each target ε is computed using the Rényi Differential Privacy (RDP) accountant. The accountant evaluates the RDP guarantee at a range of Rényi orders α∈ {1.1, 1.2, …, 1.9, 2, 3, …, 64} and converts the tightest bound to (ε, δ)-DP with . The key parameters for the accounting are:
• Steps per round:  (with 3 local epochs: 75 steps per round)
• Total steps: 10 rounds × 75 steps = 750 gradient steps
• Per-step RDP:  for Gaussian mechanism at order α
• Composition: Total RDP = 750 × per-step RDP (linear composition under RDP)
• Conversion: ; minimized over all

4.6 Privacy Attack Setup
4.6.1 Membership Inference Attack Configuration
The membership inference attack (MIA) follows the shadow-model paradigm described in Section 3.7.1 of the Methodology. The attack is configured as follows:
Shadow Models: Three shadow models are trained, each on a random subset of the training data containing up to . Each shadow model is trained for 5 epochs with the same optimizer configuration as the target model (SGD, lr = 0.01, weight , batch size 32).
Feature Extraction: For each sample presented to a shadow model, the following features are extracted: (1) the model’s prediction value, (2) the absolute prediction error, (3) the L2 norm of the user embedding, (4) the L2 norm of the item embedding, (5) the dot product of the user and item embeddings, and (6) the full user and item embedding vectors (flattened). This yields a feature vector of dimension 5 + 2×64 = 133.
Attack Classifier: A Random Forest classifier with 50 estimators (scikit-learn defaults otherwise) is trained on the labeled feature vectors from all three shadow models. The classifier learns to discriminate between member (training) and non-member (test) samples.
Evaluation: The trained classifier is evaluated on 100 member and 100 non-member samples drawn from the target model. Performance is measured by AUC (area under the ROC curve) and classification accuracy. An AUC of 0.5 corresponds to random guessing (no privacy leakage).
4.6.2 Model Inversion Attack Configuration
The model inversion attack attempts to reconstruct a user’s top-K preferred items from the trained model. For each of 50 randomly sampled target users:
• All 1,682 items are scored using the model’s prediction function.
• The top-10 items by predicted score are selected as the “reconstructed” preferences.
• These are compared against the ground-truth top-10 items (determined by actual training ratings).
• A user is considered “successfully attacked” if at least 20% (i.e., 2 out of 10) of the reconstructed items overlap with the true top-10.
The attack success rate is reported as the fraction of the 50 target users for whom the overlap threshold is met.

4.7 Experiment Configurations
The experimental design consists of four experiment groups, totaling 30 individual experiment runs. Each federated learning configuration is repeated with three random seeds (42, 123, 456) to report mean ±standard deviation. The following subsections describe each group.
4.7.1 Experiment 1: Centralized Baseline
A centralized model is trained on the full 80,000-interaction training set for 50 epochs with batch size 64, learning rate 0.01, and no differential privacy. This configuration serves as the upper bound for recommendation accuracy and provides the reference point against which federated learning degradation is measured. The centralized model uses the same MatrixFactorization architecture with embedding dimension 64, totaling 167,936 parameters.
4.7.2 Experiment 2: DP Budget Sweep (RQ1)
Federated learning is run with 100 clients, 10 communication rounds, and α = 0.5 (moderately non-IID) for five privacy budgets: ε∈ {∞, 8, 4, 2, 1}. Each of the five configurations is repeated with three seeds, yielding 15 experiments. The ε = ∞ configuration disables DP-SGD entirely (no clipping, no noise). This experiment group isolates the effect of differential privacy on recommendation quality while holding the data distribution constant.
4.7.3 Experiment 3: Privacy Attack Evaluation (RQ2)
For each of the five DP budgets in Experiment 2 (using seed 42), the trained model is subjected to both the membership inference attack and the model inversion attack. This yields five attack evaluation configurations. The attacks are applied to the final global model after round 10. This experiment group assesses whether increasing the DP guarantee effectively reduces the success rate of practical privacy attacks.
4.7.4 Experiment 4: Heterogeneity Sweep (RQ3)
Federated learning is run with 100 clients, 10 rounds, no differential privacy (ε = ∞), and three Dirichlet concentration parameters: α∈ {0.1, 0.5, 1.0}. Each configuration is repeated with three seeds, yielding 9 experiments (of which 3 overlap with the DP sweep at α = 0.5, ε = ∞). This experiment group isolates the effect of data heterogeneity on federated learning performance by removing the confound of differential privacy.

4.9 Evaluation Protocol
4.9.1 Recommendation Metric Computation
Recommendation metrics are computed using the full item catalog scoring approach. For each sampled test user (up to 100 users), the model scores all 1,682 items by computing the dot product of the user’s embedding with every item embedding. Items are ranked by predicted score in descending order, and the top-10 items are selected. The following metrics are then computed:
NDCG@10 uses the original 1–5 ratings as graded relevance scores. , normalized by the ideal DCG (IDCG) obtained from a perfect ranking of the user’s items.
Hit@10 is a binary indicator: 1 if any item in the top-10 has a ground-truth rating ≥ 4.0, and 0 otherwise. The average across users gives the hit rate.
Precision@10 is the fraction of items in the top-10 with a ground-truth rating ≥ 4.0.
Recall@10 is the fraction of all items with a ground-truth rating ≥ 4.0 that appear in the top-10.
Recommendation metrics are computed at key checkpoint rounds (1, 5, and 10) rather than every round, as scoring all items for all users is computationally more expensive than the regression metrics.


4.9.2 Regression Metric Computation
MSE and MAE are computed over the full 20,000-interaction test set every round. These metrics evaluate the model’s ability to predict exact rating values rather than its ranking performance, providing a complementary view of model quality.
4.9.3 Privacy Metric Computation
Three privacy metrics are collected for each attack evaluation:
MIA AUC: Area under the ROC curve for the membership inference classifier. Values near 0.5 indicate that the classifier performs no better than random, meaning the model does not leak membership information.
MIA Accuracy: Classification accuracy of the attack classifier on the balanced evaluation set (100 members, 100 non-members). Baseline accuracy is 0.5.
Inversion Top-K Accuracy: Fraction of target users whose top-10 preferences were successfully reconstructed with ≥ 20% overlap.
4.10 Results Storage and Analysis Pipeline
4.10.1 Result Serialization Format
Each experiment produces two output files:
JSON File: A comprehensive record containing the experiment identifier, timestamp, full configuration dictionary, per-round metrics (train loss, test MSE, MAE, and recommendation metrics at checkpoint rounds), aggregation statistics (number of participating clients, total samples), and final metrics. Files are named following the convention: dp_{epsilon}_alpha_{alpha}_dim_64_clients_100_seed_{seed}.json.
CSV Summary: A tabular summary with one row per round, suitable for quick inspection and import into spreadsheet tools. Columns include round number, train loss, all six evaluation metrics, and a final summary row.
Attack evaluation results are consolidated into a single JSON file (attack_evaluation_summary.json) containing MIA AUC, MIA accuracy, and inversion accuracy for each ε configuration.
4.10.2 Analysis and Visualization
A dedicated analysis script (comprehensive_analysis.py) reads all result JSON files and generates publication-quality figures. The script computes mean and standard deviation across the three seeds for each configuration and produces the following visualizations:
Convergence plots: Training loss versus communication round for all ε values, showing learning dynamics under different privacy constraints.
Accuracy vs. ε plots: NDCG@10 and Hit@10 as a function of the privacy budget, with error bars representing ±1 standard deviation.
Accuracy loss plot: Relative accuracy degradation (%) from the no-DP baseline as ε decreases.
Heterogeneity plots: NDCG@10 and Hit@10 as a function of α, isolating the effect of data distribution on FL performance.
Attack effectiveness plots: MIA AUC and accuracy versus ε, demonstrating the effectiveness of DP in mitigating privacy attacks.
All figures are rendered at 1200×800 pixel resolution using Seaborn’s darkgrid style, with clear legends, axis labels, and error bars. Figures are saved as PNG files in the figures/ directory.
4.11 Mobile Client Integration
4.11.1 Flutter Application Architecture
A companion mobile application built with Flutter (Dart) demonstrates the feasibility of on-device federated learning. The application targets Android (minimum API level 21) and includes the following components:
Matrix Factorization Model (Dart): A pure-Dart implementation of the same matrix factorization architecture used in the Python experiments. The model performs forward passes, computes gradients via manual backpropagation, and applies SGD updates entirely on-device without any native ML framework dependency.
FL Client Service: Manages the federated learning lifecycle: registration with the server, downloading global model parameters, local training, and uploading updated parameters. Communication uses HTTP with JSON payloads; model parameters are serialized as base64-encoded Float32 arrays for cross-platform compatibility.
Resource Monitor: Tracks battery level, battery drain rate, memory usage, and device information during training. These metrics quantify the on-device computational cost of federated learning.

User Interface: A single-screen interface displaying the server URL input, connection status, training logs (last 50 messages), per-round metrics, and resource consumption. Users can initiate training and export results.
4.11.2 Server–Client Communication Protocol
The distributed deployment uses a FastAPI server that exposes the following endpoints:
POST /register — Client registration with a unique client ID.
POST /init-model — Server-side model initialization with specified dimensions (num_users, num_items, embedding_dim).
POST /global-params-json — Download current global model parameters as base64-encoded tensors.
POST /upload-params-json — Upload local model updates along with the client’s sample count for weighted aggregation.
Model parameters are transmitted as JSON objects containing the parameter name, tensor shape, data type, and base64-encoded Float32 byte arrays. For the 167,936-parameter model, a single parameter upload is approximately 650 KB.


4.12 Reproducibility Measures
Several measures ensure full reproducibility of all reported results:
Fixed Random Seeds: All stochastic operations—PyTorch weight initialization, NumPy Dirichlet sampling, data shuffling, and train/test splitting—use fixed seeds. Each experiment configuration is run with seeds {42, 123, 456}, and results are reported as mean ± standard deviation.
Deterministic Data Pipeline: The train–test split uses a fixed permutation, and the Dirichlet partitioning is seeded, so identical client data partitions are produced across runs with the same seed.
Single-Script Execution: The complete experiment pipeline—data loading, partitioning, training, evaluation, and analysis—is encapsulated in run_complete_experiment.py, which can be executed end-to-end without manual intervention.
Result Persistence: Every experiment writes a self-contained JSON file with the full configuration and all per-round metrics, enabling independent verification and re-analysis.
Version Control: All code, configuration, data processing scripts, and result files are maintained in a Git repository.
4.13 Summary
The experimental setup provides a controlled, reproducible, and comprehensive framework for investigating accuracy–privacy trade-offs in federated learning for mobile recommendation. The design systematically varies the privacy budget ε (RQ1), evaluates privacy attacks across all DP configurations (RQ2), and examines data heterogeneity via the Dirichlet parameter α (RQ3). A total of 30 experiment runs—spanning 1 centralized baseline, 15 DP sweep configurations, 5 attack evaluations, and 9 heterogeneity sweep configurations—are executed with triple-seed averaging to ensure statistical reliability. The in-memory simulation architecture enables rapid iteration while remaining functionally equivalent to a real distributed deployment, which is demonstrated by the companion Flutter mobile client.
Results and Discussions
5.1 Overview
This chapter presents the results of the 30 experiments conducted to evaluate the accuracy–privacy trade-offs in federated learning for mobile movie recommendation. The results are organized around the three research questions: Section 5.2 establishes the centralized baseline; Section 5.3 examines how the differential privacy budget ε affects recommendation quality (RQ1); Section 5.4 evaluates the effectiveness of privacy attacks under varying DP configurations (RQ2); and Section 5.5 analyzes the impact of data heterogeneity on federated learning performance (RQ3). Section 5.6 provides a cross-cutting discussion of the key findings. All federated results report the mean ± standard deviation across three random seeds (42, 123, 456) unless otherwise noted.
5.2 Centralized Baseline
The centralized model, trained on the full 80,000-interaction training set for 50 epochs, serves as the upper bound for recommendation accuracy. Table 8 presents the final metrics at epoch 50.
Table 8: Centralized Baseline Results (Epoch 50)
Metric

0.0339

1.106

1.865
The centralized model achieves an NDCG@10 of 0.225 and a Hit@10 of 0.38, meaning that 38% of test users receive at least one relevant recommendation (rating ≥ 4.0) in their top-10 list. The training loss converges from 13.74 at epoch 1 to 1.87 at epoch 50, demonstrating that the matrix factorization model effectively learns the user–item interaction patterns in a centralized setting.
Notably, the best recommendation metrics are observed at epoch 30 (NDCG@10 = 0.284, Hit@10 = 0.46) rather than epoch 50, indicating mild overfitting in the later training stages. The MSE decreases monotonically (from 13.62 at epoch 10 to 2.05 at epoch 50), suggesting that while the model continues to improve its rating predictions, the ranking quality degrades slightly due to overfitting to the training distribution. These centralized results establish the reference point against which all federated learning experiments are compared.

The results reveal a clear, monotonic accuracy–privacy trade-off. As the privacy budget ε decreases from ∞(no privacy) to 1 (very strong privacy), NDCG@10 declines from 0.0539 to 0.0467, representing a 13.4% relative degradation. Hit@10 follows a similar pattern, dropping from 6.33% to 5.33% (a 15.8% relative decrease). These trends are visualized in Figure 15.

Figure 8: NDCG@10 and Hit@10 as a function of DP budget (ε). The red dashed line indicates the centralized baseline. Error bars show ±1 standard deviation across 3 seeds.
Figure 1 illustrates the substantial gap between the centralized baseline (NDCG@10 = 0.225, Hit@10 = 0.38) and all federated configurations. Even without differential privacy (ε = ∞), the federated model achieves only NDCG@10 = 0.054, representing a 76% degradation from the centralized baseline. This large federated-to-centralized gap is the dominant factor in the overall performance loss, with DP adding a comparatively modest additional penalty of approximately 13–16% relative to the no-DP federated baseline.

All configurations exhibit a relative accuracy loss exceeding 76%, far above the 5% target threshold typically desired in production systems. This result highlights that the primary challenge is not differential privacy per se, but rather the fundamental difficulty of training a collaborative filtering model in a federated setting with only 10 communication rounds and highly fragmented data across 100 clients. Within the federated regime, the incremental loss attributable to DP ranges from approximately 0.3 percentage points (ε = 8) to 3.2 percentage points (ε = 1) in NDCG@10.

The regression metrics reveal a much more dramatic impact of differential privacy than the ranking metrics. The test MSE increases by two orders of magnitude from ε = ∞ (MSE = 13.7) to ε = 1 (MSE = 1,219.8). The training loss at ε = 1 reaches 50,548, compared to 13.7 without DP, indicating that the massive Gaussian noise (σ = 75.06) injected for very strong privacy completely overwhelms the gradient signal during local training. Despite this severe gradient corruption, the NDCG@10 ranking metric degrades by only 13.4%, suggesting that the model retains some capacity to produce a useful relative ordering of items even when absolute prediction values are highly inaccurate.
5.3.4 Training Convergence
Figure 3 depicts the training loss and NDCG@10 convergence across communication rounds for each DP configuration.

Figure 10: Training loss convergence (left) and NDCG@10 convergence (right) over 10 communication rounds for each ε configuration. Note the dramatically different loss scales for high-noise configurations.
The convergence plots reveal striking differences in training dynamics across privacy budgets. The no-DP configuration (ε = ∞) and relaxed-privacy configuration (ε = 8) maintain relatively stable training losses around 13.7 and 32–38 respectively. In contrast, the strong-privacy configurations (ε = 2 and ε = 1) exhibit steadily increasing training losses across rounds, reaching 4,390 and 50,548 by round 10. This divergence indicates that the noise magnitude at ε≤ 2 exceeds the gradient signal, causing the model parameters to drift rather than converge.
The NDCG@10 convergence panel shows that recommendation quality is measured at rounds 1, 5, and 10. All configurations exhibit similar NDCG@10 values at the measured checkpoints (ranging from 0.04 to 0.065), despite the vastly different loss magnitudes. This finding confirms that the ranking metric is more robust to DP noise than the regression metrics, as the relative ordering of item predictions is partially


preserved even when absolute values are perturbed.
5.4 RQ2: Effectiveness of Privacy Attacks

The membership inference attack is remarkably ineffective across all configurations, even without differential privacy. At ε = ∞ (no DP), the MIA achieves an AUC of only 0.548 and accuracy of 54.5%, barely exceeding the random baseline of 0.5. This indicates that the federated learning process itself provides a degree of inherent privacy protection: because each client’s data only influences the global model indirectly through aggregation, the model does not memorize individual training samples as strongly as a centralized model would.
With even moderate DP (ε = 4), the MIA AUC drops to 0.502, which is statistically indistinguishable from random guessing. At ε = 1, the AUC falls to 0.486, slightly below 0.5, meaning the attack classifier performs worse than random. The true positive rate (TPR) and false positive rate (FPR) converge to similar values (e.g., TPR = 0.64, FPR = 0.64 at ε = 2), confirming that the classifier cannot distinguish members from non-members.
