The model inversion attack fails completely across all configurations, achieving a 0% success rate for all 50 target users at every privacy level. Not a single user’s top-10 preferences could be reconstructed with even 20% overlap (i.e., 2 out of 10 items). This result holds even at ε = ∞ (no DP), indicating that the federated


model’s predictions do not sufficiently encode individual user preferences to enable this type of attack. The combination of data fragmentation across 100 clients, limited communication rounds (10), and the relatively low model capacity (64-dimensional embeddings) collectively prevent the model from memorizing individual user preference patterns.
5.4.3 Combined Privacy Attack Assessment

Figure 11: Privacy attack effectiveness as a function of DP budget. Left: Membership Inference Attack AUC and accuracy (dashed line = random baseline at 0.5). Right: Model Inversion Attack top-K accuracy (all values are 0.0).
Figure 4 provides a comprehensive visualization of both attack types. The MIA panel shows a clear monotonic relationship between ε and attack success: as privacy protection increases (lower ε), the AUC converges toward 0.5. The model inversion panel is uniformly zero across all configurations. These results lead to an important practical conclusion: for this recommendation task and model architecture, the federated learning protocol itself provides strong baseline privacy protection. Differential privacy provides formal guarantees and additional marginal protection, reducing MIA AUC from 0.548 to 0.500, but the practical privacy risk is already minimal without DP.
5.5 RQ3: Impact of Data Heterogeneity on Federated Learning

Figure 12: NDCG@10 and Hit@10 as a function of data heterogeneity (α). Error bars show ±1 standard deviation across 3 seeds. No differential privacy is applied (ε = ∞).
The heterogeneity sweep yields a striking result: data heterogeneity has virtually no measurable impact on federated learning performance in this experimental configuration. NDCG@10 remains constant at 0.054 and Hit@10 at 0.063 across all three α values. The standard deviations across seeds are identical, and the differences between α = 0.1 and α = 1.0 are within the fourth decimal place. As shown in Figure 12, the error bars overlap almost entirely.
5.5.2 Interpretation of Heterogeneity Robustness
Several factors explain the observed robustness to data heterogeneity:
Full client participation: All 100 clients participate in every round. The FedAvg aggregation with sample-count weighting effectively averages out local biases when all clients contribute, regardless of how skewed individual client distributions are. In contrast, heterogeneity effects are typically more pronounced with partial client participation.
Limited convergence regime: With only 10 communication rounds, the model has not converged sufficiently for heterogeneity-induced client drift to manifest. Client drift (divergence between local optima) accumulates over many rounds of training; in the early stages of training, all clients are learning similar broad patterns regardless of their data distribution.
Low model capacity: The matrix factorization model with 64-dimensional embeddings has limited capacity to overfit to individual client distributions. A more complex model (e.g., a deep neural network) would be more susceptible to client drift caused by non-IID data.
Sparse interaction data: With 80,000 interactions distributed across 100 clients (average 800 per client), the per-client data is too sparse for local models to develop strongly divergent update directions, especially within 3 local epochs.
5.6 Client Data Distribution Analysis


To provide context for the federated learning results, Figure 13 visualizes the distribution of training samples across clients and Figure 14 shows the aggregation statistics across rounds.

Figure 13: Distribution of training samples per client under Dirichlet partitioning (α = 0.5). Left: histogram showing the frequency of client dataset sizes. Right: box plot showing the median, quartiles, and outliers.
Figure 6 reveals that the Dirichlet partitioning with α = 0.5 produces a skewed distribution of client dataset sizes, with a mean of approximately 140 samples per client. The distribution ranges from roughly 60 to 280 samples, with the interquartile range spanning approximately 110 to 155. One outlier client holds roughly 280 samples. This distribution is representative of real-world mobile deployments where user engagement levels vary substantially.

Figure 14: Aggregation statistics over 10 communication rounds. Left: number of participating clients per round (constant at 48). Right: total samples aggregated per round (stable at approximately 6,700).


Figure 7 confirms consistent aggregation behavior across all 10 rounds. The number of participating clients remains constant at 48 per round, and the total sample count is stable at approximately 6,700 per round. This consistency ensures that performance variations observed across rounds are attributable to model learning dynamics rather than fluctuations in client participation or data availability.
5.7 Recommendation Metrics Over Training Rounds

Figure 15: Recommendation metrics (Hit@10, Precision@10, Recall@10) tracked over 10 training rounds for the no-DP federated configuration.
Figure 8 tracks the evolution of recommendation metrics over the training rounds for the baseline federated configuration (no DP). Hit@10 remains stable at approximately 8% for the first 8 rounds before dropping to 6% in the final two rounds. Precision@10 and Recall@10 remain consistently low throughout training (approximately 0.8% and 0.3% respectively), reflecting the difficulty of the recommendation task with such sparse data and limited training rounds.
The slight decline in Hit@10 during the last two rounds may indicate the onset of overfitting at the federated level, where continued aggregation of locally-overfit models degrades the global model’s generalization ability. This observation parallels the overfitting pattern noted in the centralized baseline, although it manifests at a much earlier stage in the federated setting due to the limited data per client.

The most significant finding across all experiments is the substantial performance gap between centralized and federated learning. Table 15 summarizes this gap.

The federated model retains only 24% of the centralized model’s NDCG@10 and 17% of its Hit@10. This degradation stems from several compounding factors: (1) data fragmentation across 100 clients reduces the effective training set size per client to approximately 800 interactions; (2) only 10 communication rounds limits the total amount of model refinement possible; (3) the matrix factorization model requires coordinated updates to both user and item embeddings, which is inherently challenging when user data is distributed; and (4) the sparse nature of the MovieLens 100K dataset (93.7% sparsity) exacerbates the data scarcity problem at the client level.
5.8.2 Privacy–Accuracy Trade-off Analysis
When the analysis is restricted to the federated regime, the DP-induced accuracy loss is moderate and follows a predictable pattern. Table 16 isolates the incremental impact of DP by comparing each εconfiguration against the no-DP federated baseline.
Table 16: Incremental DP Impact (Relative to ε = ∞ Federated Baseline)

The results suggest that ε = 8 offers the best privacy–accuracy trade-off for this task: it reduces MIA AUC from 0.548 to 0.534 (a 2.6% improvement in privacy protection) while incurring only a 0.9% accuracy loss. At ε = 4, the MIA AUC reaches 0.502 (effectively random), providing near-complete privacy protection at the cost of 11.1% NDCG@10 degradation. Configurations with ε≤ 2 offer diminishing returns: the MIA is already at random performance at ε = 4, so further tightening the privacy budget yields no additional practical privacy benefit while continuing to erode accuracy.
5.8.3 Practical Implications for Mobile Deployment
The experimental results yield several practical implications for deploying federated recommendation systems on mobile devices:
Moderate DP is sufficient: A privacy budget of ε = 4–8 renders membership inference attacks completely ineffective while preserving most of the federated model’s accuracy. Very strong privacy (ε = 1) offers no additional practical protection and is therefore unnecessary for this use case.
Federated learning provides inherent privacy: Even without DP, the federated aggregation process limits privacy leakage to marginal levels (MIA AUC = 0.548). Model inversion attacks are completely ineffective at all privacy levels, indicating that individual user preferences cannot be reconstructed from the federated model.
More communication rounds needed: The 76% accuracy gap between centralized and federated training indicates that 10 communication rounds are insufficient for convergence. Increasing the number of rounds, combined with techniques like learning rate scheduling and momentum-based optimizers, could substantially narrow this gap.
Heterogeneity is not a bottleneck: The FedAvg algorithm with full client participation is robust to data heterogeneity in this configuration, suggesting that non-IID data distributions are not a primary concern for mobile recommendation deployment.
5.9 Summary of Key Findings
Table 17 consolidates the key quantitative findings across all three research questions.
Table 17: Summary of Key Findings
Research Question
Key Finding
Key Metric

Moderate trade-off: 13% NDCG loss from ε=∞to ε=1 within the federated regime
NDCG@10: 0.054 → 0.047
RQ1: Federated Gap
Dominant factor: 76% NDCG loss from centralized to federated, dwarfing DP effects

RQ2: MIA

RQ2: Inversion

RQ3: Heterogeneity
No impact: α from 0.1 to 1.0 has negligible effect; FedAvg is robust with full participation
NDCG@10: ≈0.054 for all α
In summary, the results demonstrate that federated learning for mobile recommendation faces a significant accuracy challenge compared to centralized training, but this gap is primarily attributable to the limited communication budget and data fragmentation—not to differential privacy. DP at moderate levels (ε = 4–8) provides robust formal privacy guarantees while imposing only a small additional accuracy penalty. Privacy attacks are largely ineffective even without DP, and data heterogeneity does not materially affect performance in this configuration. These findings suggest that the primary avenue for improving federated recommendation systems lies in addressing the convergence challenge through more communication rounds, better optimization strategies, and potentially more expressive model architectures.



Conclusion
6.1 Summary of Findings
This research empirically evaluated the trade-offs between utility and privacy in federated recommendation systems using the MovieLens 100K dataset. The comprehensive experimental analysis yields three primary conclusions regarding the deployability of private federated learning on mobile devices.
First, differential privacy proves to be a viable protection mechanism that does not prohibitively sacrifice utility. The results demonstrate that a privacy budget in the range of epsilon in [4, 8] offers an optimal trade-off, providing rigorous formal privacy guarantees while maintaining recommendation accuracy comparable to non-private baselines. Stricter privacy settings (lower epsilon) result in a sharp utility drop, identifying this range as the critical operating point for practical deployment.
Second, the vulnerability analysis reveals that the sparsity of recommendation data acts as a natural privacy defense. Model Inversion Attacks (MIA) failed completely across all experimental configurations, achieving a 0% success rate. Membership Inference Attacks similarly showed limited effectiveness even without differential privacy, suggesting that gradient updates in matrix factorization models leak significantly less sensitive information than those in dense neural networks for image or text classification.
Third, the system demonstrated unexpected robustness to data heterogeneity. Contrasting with common challenges in federated learning, variations in the non-IID degree (controlled by the Dirichlet parameter alpha) had negligible impact on model convergence and final accuracy. This indicates that standard federated averaging is sufficiently robust for collaborative filtering tasks even when user data distributions are highly skewed.
6.2 Limitations
While privacy and heterogeneity concerns were effectively mitigated, the study identified that the primary performance bottleneck is the limited communication budget. The gap between federated and centralized accuracy is driven more by the constrained number of training rounds than by privacy noise or data distribution. Consequently, future work should prioritize communication-efficient optimization strategies, such as gradient compression and quantization, to enable longer training durations on resource-constrained mobile devices.
Additionally, while the current attack models were ineffective, future research should explore more sophisticated threats tailored to sparse data, such as property inference attacks, to ensure comprehensive security in production environments.
Future Work
Future research efforts will aim at making the model more robust to a larger class of adversarial threats that are present in federated learning environments. This includes model poisoning attacks, in which malicious clients send data updates to other clients that are invalid or not converged to disrupt the global model convergence and accuracy, attacks against membership inference, which uses model output or gradient to learn user preference on private information, which is used in personalized recommendation systems, such as personal movie watching history, and attacks against model inversion to recover private data trained by the model. To battle against these, future defenses may include robust aggregation mechanisms such as geometric median or trimmed mean, client reputation scoring mechanisms that will be able to identify and isolate the anomalous behaviours in norms of update deviation and contribution from historical record.
Strengthening of privacy mechanisms remain equally important, especially, with respect to complex and heterogeneous network environments (where the distribution of data is not IID in nature) (e.g., label skew, quantity skew and feature shift), in resource constrained, mobile devices which vary in computational power and connectivity. Promising integrations are blockchain technology to tamper proof the traceability model update by immutability and creation ledger plus comprehensive audit trails - so the potential for notion of smart contracts for incentive mechanisms for honest participation. Complementing this, advanced mechanism of privacy with adaptive noise calibration such as entropy-adaptive methods with dynamic adjustment of noise based on the local uncertainty of the data could better balance between the utility to preserve the recommendation accuracy and the risk of information leakage. Not only these enhancements will ensure that the framework evolves keeping up with the threats. strict rules such as GDPR, CCPA etc. while scaling up to large scale deployments will be complied.
A major direction for progress is to look for homomorphic encryption schemes that are more different from partially homomorphic encryption (e.g Paillier for addition operations) as to fully homomorphic encryption (e.g CKKS or BFV schemes for both operations addition and multiplication on ciphertexts). These support secure model aggregation protocols in which the central server performs computing directly on encrypted client updates without ever having to decrypt in this way evading the eavesdropping attack as well as man in the middle attacks as well as exposing server-side data during transmission. Despite the computational cost of the ciphertexts, there are optimisations that could be employed to make HE feasible in the context of mobile federated scenarios, e.g. packing of multiple values in single ciphertexts and bootstrapping reductions.
Complementing HE, advanced client selection algorithms, which includes adaptive strategies for adapting with the actual-time network conditions, contribution-based strategies for evaluating the quality of the gradient and the nom similarity, fairness-aware strategy for ensuring a fair representation in user demographics, and grouping strategies based on clustering clients depending on the data distribution similarity is capable of dynamically prioritizing participants. These algorithms would look at evaluating clients using multi-dimensional factors like data quality (e.g. entropy or diversity metrics), computation power and connectivity stability, demographic diversity, etc. to optimise the accuracy vs. privacy tradeoff in heterogeneous federated learning that dominates in the mobile ecosystems. Such methods promise to modulate the stragglers by partial selection/ asynchronous update, increase the convergence speed due to high quality subsets aggregation, and increase the generalization without any breach in privacy or fairness.
Furthermore, the promising breakthrough of the server-less architectures based on fully decentralized networks, and their use of blockchain consensus mechanisms (e.g., proof-of-stake or practical Byzantine fault tolerance), peer-to-peer (P2P) communication protocols (e.g., gossip based dissemination or DHT overlays) have a significant potential to propose a more resilient and web 3.0 compatible recommender system fitted for future implementation of mobile movies. Unlike the conventional, federated learning architecture that requires a centralized server in order to coordinate the training, these are all server-less federated scenarios where all the features of coordination are distributed across the participating nodes and blockchain technology's unchangeable distributed ledgers are used to permanentlylog the updating of the results and smart contacts in order to automate the distribution of the reward based on the quality of the contribution. These systems are inherently enabled to not only support dynamic user interactions (like obviously changing preferences from binge-watching users), they may also effectively solve that cold start problem for new users (or even new movie) via democratizing the model training process through incentivize based play form a huge pool of peers. In particular they take away the reliance on centralized authority for clients to group and schedule interactions and distribute the decision-making processes through consensus-based mechanism for voting on distributed ledgers, and token-based incentivization mechanisms (like cryptocurrency reward proportional to utility of update), including promoting equitable participation, improving fault tolerance to single point failures, and scalability in edge dominated mobile environments.
Emerging directions also cope with federated continual learning frameworks - in which models may progressively adapt to changing user tastes in streaming situations without falling prey of catastrophic forgetting of prior know-how - which can be achieved by techniques such as elastic weight consolidation or replay buffer for non-IID federated streaming. This is complemented with embedding foundation models such as large language models (e.g. BERT variants or GPT architectures fine-tuned in a federated manner) in order to allow for transferability of knowledge between domains in the face of pervasive data sparsity (e.g. in niches of movie genres or in terms of emerging user demographics). Furthermore, the augmentation of methods for explainability algorithms such as SHAP values that can be locally computed on federated gradients could be exploited to offer explainable federated recommendations which have the capability to explain the importance of features (i.e. such as why a sci-fi thriller was recommend based on viewing history and metadata), thus this could encourage user trust and compliance with transparency mandates in personalized services. Additionally, multimodal fusion strategies (embedding textual user reviews, e.g. via sentiment analysis, rich metadata, e.g. genres, e.g. directors, cast and contextual signals, e.g. device type, time-of-day, location) could also be extremely powerful in the enrichment of personalization, leveraging transformer-based architectures to fuse heterogeneity streams of data and preserve privacy (local processing).
Subsequent studies will deal with the question of holistic approaches for maintaining the trade-off between the communication efficiency, good privacy guarantees and computational overhead in resource-constrained scenarios. Among the important techniques includes model compression (reducing number of parameters by 50-90%, e.g. pruning redundant neurones), quantization (neural network weights are mapped to integer type in low bit-depth computations e.g. INT8 type, for fast inference and low bandwidth) and opportunistic aggregation (neural network weights are chosen the quality of the update based on staleness or norm bounds in asynchronous rounds). These optimizations can be combined with sparse communication protocols to introduce scalable, optimal solutions for privacy-centric recommendation services for the decentralized mobile landscape with such stringent privacy budgets (e.g. ), which sub-second latency would be the result even with high heterogeneity. This balancing need an extensive empirical, in particular accuracy-privacy-trades-offs in personalized federated recommender systems whereby user behavior data is highly not-IID. Specifically, in the future, the conditions of the dynamic network and the approaches toward stragglers' mitigation need to be adapted and re-tailored in the real-world, to ensure the simultaneous guarantee of the system performance and data privacy. Moreover, understanding how these foundation models can be integrated into these frameworks without the risk of compounding some client-side resources cost or performance imbalances is set to be of a great value when it comes to building scalable and effective personalised recommendation systems.

Appendix
Deep Leakage
It is a specific type of attack where the adversary can reverse engineer the original data just by looking at the series of gradient in the Federated learning system. Since the gradient have one vector of improving the error, the attacker knows in which direction to move so that the original data can be fetched.
 INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1768946267159_l3sptseq.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET The normal machine learning works by first fixing the data point and update the weights to minimise error in Deep leakage attack. The attacker fixes the weights and gradient and then works back by updating the dummy data to match the gradient. 
In a neural network, the gradient with respect to the weights  is calculated by multiplying terms all the way back from the output to the input.
As an example, let’s see if we have a picture containing lots of pixels and we are to encode it with gradient algorithm, then it is to be noted that every pixel in the input influences the final gradient value. Therefore, we can conclude that the gradient codes, the pixel values.
Since every value in the chain of the chain rule is deterministic and perfect, leading to the initial input values. The only way to break this chain is to add some perturbation, or we can say noise to at least one of the values in between the chain rule.

The adversary is trying to track the original value using the chain rule in reverse. He will be encountered with this noise. This noise will prevent their anniversary from going into the right path back to the input in a sense, they are chasing a ghost. The mathematical mirror, which was helping the adversary to track back. The whole input value is no shattered, and he will be led to an image, which is meaningless or at least blurry in a worst case scenario.

Oblivious Transfer Protocol
In simple words, it is a “magic trick” that allows two people (an analogy) to share information while keeping their specific choices and secrets hidden from each other. It is a fundamental building block for almost all privacy preserving technologies today. 
The Scenario
Take an example of two persons Alice and Bob. The sender in this case will be Alice and the recipient is Bob. Alice produces two envelopes one labelled zero and another one labelled the one and writes them down. Each envelope has a secret message which she writes as we shall call it Message 0 and Message 1 respectively. These messages are supposed to be confidential and one message over an envelope should be unique and the content of the mail should not be mixed and copied.
And now he, Bob, would give you the secret he wants, secret in one envelope, envelope number one, to be more precise. but he, time stands in a dilemma, he wants to see what the envelope number one contains, he wants to do what he got not, tell Alice his choice. Precisely, Bob does not want to know anything other than what is in envelope one and does want to ensure that on his part there is as little information as possible at the mind of Alice as to the envelope chosen by Bob. It is a crucial necessity in which the privacy and secrecy of choice is essential and has some cryptographic protocols or secure voting systems.
On the other hand Alice too is concerned with privacy of her messages. She is collaborative to allow Bob a moment in order to read the only one of the two secret messages namely 0 and 1 than to not both of them. She does not want Bob to know whatever was in the other envelope whatever envelope he chooses. This would ensure that information that would otherwise be spread would be kept secret and there is no message that is left to flow into the hands of Bob than there is regarding his liking.
This kind of setup will give a delicate balance: Bob will not want to be discovered by Alice, and vice versa, neither is willing to make sure that Bob is only aware of one secret and is never allowed to utilize the other. It is not that straightforward and it is a special form of a protocol with a magic graciousness, allowing Bob to choose and learn privately one and only one message and for certain the choice that will be made in Alice will not be learned privately and the same message will not be learned either. This is the basic challenge that have made the Oblivious Transfer Protocol to be developed as has been explained below. Superior privacy protective technologies and secret multi-party procedures in the digital domain are based on such protocols.
The Problem
If Bob requests envelope one then Alice knows his decision. If Alice sends them, both envelopes, then Bob will read both the messages. Hence in both of these case of the encryption the point of encryption is killed.
To elaborate, this scenario, brings two concern for privacy in a naive way of sharing secrets. Firstly if Bob asks Alice directly envelope one it is immediately obvious to Alice what message Bob is interested in. This eliminates the demand of privacy on the recipient, since his option is not hidden anymore from the sender. Bob's intention should be not public, it's prefer to him to communicate directly and the preference will be shown.
Secondly if Alice chooses to send both envelopes to Bob so that she does not know which he has chosen then unwittingly she gives Bob read access to both messages. This is violation to the privacy of the sender because Bob can now read both secrets and this is against the point of selective sharing. The basic issue in this is that either the individual sending the message knows what the receiver is slated to choose or the receiver finds out about all the secrets - in both the cases the fundamental principles behind encryption and privacy loses its meaning.
Therefore, a safe protocol is required where Bob is able to receive the only message (recieved) without the other message being exposed to Alice, and to Bob. This is the exact challenge that Oblivious Transfer Protocol is most designed to solve and ensure the privacy for both parties and doesn't leak out more information than it is meant to.
The Protocol of Oblivious Transfer
The Nadi Oblivious Transfer Protocol is a somewhat complex piece of mathematics, which is referred to as the public-key cryptography but the general logic of the process can be simply stated. We shall deconstruct and provide a description of each stage through basic analogy.
The Set-up: Suppose that Alice assembles two types of digital locks (you can imagine them to be very secure padlocks), and she puts them on a table. She also possesses a master key which can unlock the two locks but most significantly she keeps the master key and so there should be no need to hand it over to Bob. That which Alice wants to guard, are these two locks, the two possible secrets or messages (Message 0 and Message 1) that Alice wants to ensure safety over.
The Blinded Choice: Now, Bob decides what secret to wish he could see, Lock 1 (say) in specific. however, he does not want Alice to know what was this very lock. In order to conceal his choice, he makes a special computational effort to cover the lock with blindness, he is able to fantasize that he can strip vegan lock or any substance on a lock of the door so that Alice would not know that he has picked the vegan lock. Then he uncovers this indolent eye (or symbol of his indolent decision) to Alice. This way, Alice is aware whether he is on the number Lock 0 or Lock 1.
The Double Encryption: Alice gets signal by Bob/Blinded lock Because it is blind, Alice no means of telling what lock Bob is interested in. After this she transmits Message 0 and ciphers with the logic of Lock 0 only Lock 0 can decrypt and ciphers with the logic of Lock 1 Message 1. Then she can send back the two encrypted messages (the one encrypted using Lock 0 and one encrypted using Lock 1) to Bob.
The Reveal: Bob has now been given the encrypted / locked messages. This implies that, courtesy of the entirely insane calculation he has made in the past (the blinding), he has the key logic of the lock to which he is interested, (viz., Lock 1). By this, he is able to pass the opening and reading of Message 1. When trying to open Message 0 though, it is not able to do so, the calculation is not working, the decryption is not working and he can only see gibberish or other non-sensible information. Bob then goes to take his message and not that of the other.
In summary, it is a protocol like a magic trick of digital locks and keys -bob is free to unlock any of the messages that he is not aware on which message he will unlock Alice and, after that, he is not able to unlock the other message. At the same time that the other half of the secret that Alice possesses has been preserved and she will never discover what message Bob was curious of. This comes with privacy to the receiver and message sender that is highly vital to cryptography and secure systems.
Shamir’s Secret Sharing
Now let's say we have a nuclear code or some kind of top secret code, That has to be kept safe in all cost. One solution is that we handover the code to one person who will keep it secret. But this solution is not very efficient, because if something happens to that person or if the person dies, the secret goes with him and the code is lost forever. Another solution is that sharing that same secret key with someone else. This creates a problem that upon sharing the secret, The risk of the secret getting known to the public gets doubled. Similarly, if we share the secret with number of people, The risk gets even bigger by that factor. So in other words, none of the above presented solutions work.
Figure 16
There is another solution. The key has to be. Divided among n number of trusted people. Now remember that these people are trusted and these people. Have a very low risk of Revealing the secret to the public. Also, none of the members of the group know the complete secret, so the secret remains secret. However, this solution is also not efficient. Because when it's time to reveal the secret, every one of the member has to be present at the same time. So if someone gets sick or if someone is busy somewhere and is not able to attend the meeting, the secret remains hidden. (refer to Figure 16)
This is where Shamir's secret sharing scheme comes to play. We want to distribute shares to people, such that any group of people can combine their shares to reveal the secret, but any group smaller than learns absolutely nothing. This scheme can be compared with privacy, in which one or 2 absence of data does not make much effect in the whole result of the system. So if at least number of people are present at the meeting, then number of people will have almost same result when all the shares are combined to reveal the secret.

FedSlate
FedSlate is a cutting-edge framework that integrates Federated Learning (FL) with Deep Reinforcement Learning (DRL) to build privacy-preserving, long-term optimized recommender systems. Specifically, it addresses the challenge of recommending "slates"-lists of items (e.g., a YouTube homepage, a Netflix row)—rather than single items, while keeping user data decentralized on local devices.
The algorithm is designed to solve two critical problems in modern recommendation:
Privacy: Traditional recommender systems require uploading sensitive user interaction logs to a central server. FedSlate keeps raw data on the user's device.
Long-term Engagement: Traditional systems often optimize for immediate clicks (myopic). FedSlate uses Reinforcement Learning (specifically SlateQ) to optimize for long-term user satisfaction and retention.
1. Theoretical Foundation
To understand FedSlate, one must first grasp the intersection of the two domains it bridges: the Slate Recommendation problem modeled as a Markov Decision Process (MDP), and the Federated Learning paradigm.
1.1 The Slate Recommendation Problem
In real-world scenarios, systems recommend a slate (a collection of  items) rather than a single item. The user observes the slate and selects one item (or none). This selection influences the user's future state (e.g., boredom, interest shift).
We model this as an MDP defined by the tuple  :
State (  ): The user's current profile, history, and context at time .
Action  : The slate of items recommended by the system. Note that the action space is combinatorial (all possible subsets of items), which is massive.
User Choice: The user selects an item  with probability .
Reward (  ): The immediate feedback (e.g., click, watch time) received from the user's selection.
Transition: The state updates to  based on the interaction.
1.2 The Challenge of Combinatorial Action Space
Standard Q-learning attempts to learn a value function  representing the expected long-term reward of choosing action  in state . However, because  is a slate of items, the number of possible actions is , where  is the total item catalog size. This is too large to learn directly.
FedSlate leverages the SlateQ decomposition technique to solve this. SlateQ assumes the Long-Term Value (LTV) of a slate depends on the item the user actually consumes.

Here,  is the item-level Q-value: the expected long-term return if the user is presented with a slate containing item  and actually chooses item . This decomposition reduces the learning problem from the slate space to the item space.
2. Fed Slate Architecture
FedSlate operates on a client-server architecture typical of Federated Learning but adapted for RL training dynamics.

Model Inversion
Model and version is also a class of attack in Federated learning that specifically works on  privacy. In this attacks, the adversary climbs up all the  gradients that were introduced to the data in order to hide the original data points. Since gradients work on chain rule of calculus, assuming no noise has been added yet, the chain rule can be worked back from the final gradient to the original data, and this kills the whole point of privacy.
In other words, model in version only needs output scores from the deployed API. With more advanced attacks like deep leakage, the attacker, instead of simply guessing the input data from output, actually reverse engineers by “climbing” up the ladder of all the gradients in each and every level and reaches the top where the original data lies.
To understand how inversion is possible, we must look at the calculus of neural network training.
In a standard FL setting (e.g., using FedAvg), a client  holds a private dataset . The client computes the gradient of a loss function  with respect to the model parameters .
The gradient vector  sent to the server is:

Notice the direct multiplicative relationship. The gradient  is directly proportional to the input data . In a fully connected layer, the gradient of the weights connecting to the input is simply the error term scaled by the input features.

Rényi Differential Privacy
To understand Rényi  Privacy (RDP), we must first identify the friction point in standard  Privacy (DP) that necessitated its creation.
Standard ϵ-DP is a worst-case guarantee. It demands that for any possible output, the probability ratio between two neighboring datasets is strictly bounded. This is like designing a bridge assuming every single car is an overweight semi-truck. It is safe, but it is incredibly inefficient-especially when we perform the same operation thousands of times (like in training machine learning models).
The core insight of RDP is this: Instead of bounding the worst-case privacy leak, we should bound the "moments" (the average behavior of the higher powers) of the privacy leak.
This shift allows us to ignore highly improbable "catastrophic" leaks that ruin standard DP bounds, resulting in a much tighter analysis of how privacy degrades over repeated queries.
The Core Object: The Privacy Loss Random Variable
To understand RDP, we must visualize the "Privacy Loss" not as a single number, but as a shifting, random value.
Imagine two parallel universes:
Universe P: The database contains your data. (Distribution  )
Universe Q: The database does not contain your data. (Distribution  )
An attacker sees an output  (e.g., a statistic or a model weight) and tries to guess which universe they are in. The "evidence" they gain is the log-likelihood ratio, which we call the Privacy Loss Random Variable, denoted as  :

If , then . The attacker learns nothing.
If , then  is large and positive. The output  is a "fingerprint" that strongly suggests your data is present.
The Difference in Perspective Standard -DP looks at the tail of this variable. It asks: "Does  ever exceed  ?" It requires that the probability of  is very small (at most  ). It treats the privacy loss as a cliff: stay safe on the plateau, and don't fall off.
Rényi DP looks at the shape of . It asks: "What is the expectation of the exponential of  ?"Specifically, it looks at the moments of the likelihood ratio. By constraining these moments, RDP ensures that while  might get large, the probability of it doing so drops off incredibly fast-exponentially fast.
 INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1770163436185_2t1xj96e.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET 
Figure 19
The Mathematical Engine: Rényi Divergence (refer to Figure 19)
RDP quantifies the difference between Universe P and Universe Q using Rényi Divergence.
While Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence measures the average information gain (expected value of  ), Rényi divergence is a parameterized family of divergences that allows us to weigh "outliers" more heavily.
For a parameter , the Rényi Divergence  is defined as:

The Role of Alpha (  ) Think of  as a "magnifying glass" for bad events.
 : This converges to KL divergence. It measures the average privacy loss. This is often too weak for privacy guarantees because averages hide disasters.
 : This converges to the "max divergence," which is essentially pure -DP. It focuses entirely on the worst-case outcome.
Finite  (e.g., 2, 5, 20): This is the sweet spot. It penalizes high privacy loss outcomes polynomially more than low ones, but it doesn't panic over a single worst-case point like  does.

The true power of RDP-and the reason it is the standard for training Large Language Models and Deep Learning—is Composition.
In Deep Learning, we add noise to gradients thousands of times (Stochastic Gradient Descent). We need to know the total privacy loss after  steps.

Dirichlet Data Partitioning
The field of machine, learning, especially federated learning, the performance of algorithms depend upon how the data is distributed and partitioned among many different users. Because there are various types of users, generating various types of data, the data is very rarely evenly distributed or randomly distributed. So to test the algorithms despite of the heterogeneity of the data, researchers use what is called Dirichlet Data Partitioning.
The Dirichlet Distribution
The core of this method is the Dirichlet distribution, denoted as . It is a multivariate probability distribution parameterized by a vector  of positive reals. It is often referred to as a "distribution over distributions."2.1 Definition
Let  be a random vector where each  and . The vector  lies on the -dimensional simplex. The probability density function (PDF) of the Dirichlet distribution is:

(Here,  denotes the Gamma function).2.2 The Concentration Parameter (  )
In the context of data partitioning, we usually set a symmetric concentration parameter, meaning all elements of the vector are equal: . We refer to this scalar simply as .
This parameter  controls the sparsity of the sampled vectors:
 : The distribution concentrates at the center of the simplex. Sampled vectors are close to uniform .
 : The distribution is uniform over the simplex. Any valid probability vector is equally likely.
 : The distribution concentrates at the corners (vertices) of the simplex. Sampled vectors are sparse (e.g., close to one-hot vectors like  ).

The scalar  acts as the heterogeneity knob.
Extreme Heterogeneity (  ). When  is small (e.g., 0.1 or 0.5 ), the Dirichlet distribution pushes probability mass to the edges.
Result: For a specific class , the vector  will likely have one or two large values and many values near zero.
Interpretation: Class  is found almost exclusively on one or two clients. Client  might possess data for only 2 out of 10 classes. This is a difficult setting for Federated Learning because the local objective functions of the clients are very different from the global objective.
Moderate Heterogeneity (  ) The proportions are random but cover the simplex.
Result: Clients have varying amounts of each class, but usually have some representation of most classes.
IID Setting When is large (e.g., 100 or 1000 ), the distribution converges to the mean.
Result: .
Interpretation: Every client gets roughly the same amount of every class. This effectively reconstructs a random shuffle (IID).

Multi-Objective Optimization
In a standard machine learning problem, we optimize a scalar value (the loss). In the FL+DP context, we are optimizing a vector of functions. We want to minimize the error while simultaneously minimizing the privacy loss (or maximizing the privacy budget tightness).
We define the vector of objectives  :
Figure 23

Because these objectives conflict (improving privacy hurts error), there is no single "best" solution. Instead, we look for Pareto Optimal solutions.
A solution  is Pareto optimal if there is no other solution  such that  is better than  in all objectives. The set of all such optimal trade-offs forms the Pareto Front.
Heterogeneity as an MOO Problem
In Federated Learning, MOO is not just about Privacy vs. Accuracy. It is often about Client Fairness.If Client A has clean data and Client B has noisy data, a single global model might work well for A but fail for B .
We can reformulate the FL problem as minimizing the worst-case loss among all clients:

Or treating each client's loss as a separate objective in the MOO vector:

Adding  Privacy adds yet another dimension to this vector, as different clients may have different privacy preferences.
Solving the MOO Problem in FL+DP
Scalarization (Linearization)
The simplest approach is to convert the vector problem back into a scalar problem by assigning weights  to each objective.

In the context of DP, this often manifests as a constrained optimization problem:  subject to This is usually solved using Lagrange multipliers. The "price" of privacy becomes a regularization term. However, finding the correct  is difficult and requires extensive tuning.
Gradient-Based MOO (MGDA)
A more advanced method uses the Multiple Gradient Descent Algorithm (MGDA). This method looks at the gradients of the conflicting objectives and attempts to find a direction that improves all of them (or at least improves one without hurting the others).
Let  be the gradient of the loss and  be the gradient of the privacy penalty (or the gradient of a specific client's loss).
If the angle between  and  is less than , we can step in the average direction to improve both.
If the angle is greater than , the objectives conflict. Moving down  increases .
MGDA computes a direction  that is the minimum norm element in the convex hull of the gradients. This ensures the model converges to a Pareto stationary point.
 INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1769541933077_ggmnj7s8.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET  INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1769541933077_62i04728.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET 
Figure 24
Advanced Application: Personalized Differential Privacy (refer to Figure 24)
One of the most powerful applications of MOO in this field is Personalized  Privacy.
In a standard setting, every client gets the same privacy budget . However, some users might be less sensitive about their data than others.
User A: "I don't care who sees my data." (Low privacy requirement, contributes high utility).
User B: "My data is highly sensitive." (High privacy requirement, contributes noise).
If we force User A to have high privacy, we waste their potential to improve the model. If we force User B to have low privacy, we violate their trust.
We treat this as a Multi-Objective problem where we optimize the global accuracy while satisfying a vector of privacy constraints .
The optimization finds a model  such that:

This allows the system to extract more "signal" from less privacy-sensitive users while strictly protecting the sensitive ones, pushing the Pareto front of the global model further into the high-accuracy/high-privacy zone.
Poisson Subsampling
In the world of machine learning today, there are two vital limitations that are often in conflict with each other: to be able to train machine learning models on huge and distributed data sets and to protect the privacy of people whose data is being circulated around. Federated Learning (FL) addresses the distributed nature of data by decentralizing the process of training data on the devices, and the term  Privacy (DP) represents a powerful mathematical tool to quantify and limit the level of data-leakage to privacy.
Poisson Subsampling is an important connecting link between these two fields. It is a method of randomized sampling, when used to select the participants or bits of data in an algorithm, which drastically increases the privacy guarantees of the system. This phenomenon is referred to as privacy amplification.
In the light of FL, Poisson subsampling transforms the client selection procedure from deterministic or fixed size procedure to probabilistic ensuring that there is less statistical dependency of the global model on an individual user data.
Mathematical Definition
Let  be a dataset (or a set of clients in FL) of size .In Poisson Subsampling, a subset  is constructed by making an independent binary decision for each element .
For a sampling probability , each element is included in the sample  according to a Bernoulli trial:

This decision is independent for all .
Properties
Variable Sample Size: Unlike fixed-batch sampling where , in Poisson subsampling, the sample size  is a random variable following a Binomial distribution:The expected sample size is .
Independence: The inclusion of client  does not influence the probability of including client . This independence is mathematically vital for simplifying the composition analysis in  Privacy.
 INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1769543040047_letwa7na.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET 
Figure 25
Matthew Effect (refer to Figure 25)
The Matthew Effect arises because the "cost" of privacy (the noise and clipping) is not paid equally by all classes. It is determined by the Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR).
The Catalyst: Differential Privacy
To prevent the server or an adversary from reconstructing a specific user's data from their gradient update , we apply  Privacy. In the context of FL, this is typically Client-Level DP.
To satisfy DP, two operations are performed on the client's update before aggregation:
Gradient Clipping: The update  is clipped to a maximum norm  to bound the sensitivity of the aggregation function.

Noise Injection: Random noise (usually Gaussian) is added to the sum of updates.

Here,  is the noise multiplier determined by the privacy budget .
Mechanism of Matthew Effect
A. The Impact of Noise on Minority Classes
The global update is a summation of signal plus noise.

For Majority Classes: There are many clients contributing gradients pointing in the same direction. The sum of these gradients grows linearly with the number of clients (  ), while the noise variance remains constant (or grows slower than the signal). The signal overwhelms the noise.
For Minority Classes: There are very few clients contributing gradients. The sum of their gradients is small. The DP noise, which is isotropic (added equally in all directions), can easily have a magnitude larger than the minority signal.
Result: The model effectively "unlearns" or fails to learn the minority classes because their gradients are drowned out by static, whereas the majority classes remain robust.
B. The Impact of Clipping (Gradient Bias)
Gradient clipping is particularly detrimental to "hard" examples or outliers, which often correspond to minority data.
To learn a rare pattern, a model often generates a large gradient (high error implies a large update vector).
DP requires clipping this vector to norm .
 INCLUDEPICTURE "https://files.gpai.app/visuals/dxv_1769521849418_a99ss6vp.png" \* MERGEFORMATINET This clipping introduces bias. The direction of the update is preserved, but its magnitude is crushed. When combined with noise, a clipped small vector is statistically indistinguishable from pure noise. 
Figure 26


Mathematical Formulation of the Disparity
We can quantify this effect by looking at the convergence bounds of ly Private Stochastic Gradient Descent (DP-SGD).
Let  be the loss function. The convergence error bound in a convex setting for DP-SGD is often expressed as:

Where:
 is the number of iterations.
 is the dimension of the model.
 is the effective sample size for the specific feature/class.
 is the noise scale.
Notice the second term (the privacy cost): .
For the Majority:  is large. The term  becomes negligible. The privacy cost is close to zero.
For the Minority:  is small. The term  is large. The privacy cost dominates the convergence bound.
This creates a feedback loop. As the model improves on the majority, the feature representations optimize for those classes. The gradients for the minority classes become even more "anomalous" (larger magnitude) relative to the majority features, leading to more aggressive clipping, further reducing their signal.
Visualizing the Divergence
The Matthew Effect is best observed by comparing the accuracy gap between groups before and after applying DP.

Codebase Structure
Table of Contents
[Code Structure](#code-structure)
[Folder Organization](#folder-organization)
[Running the Code](#running-the-code)
[Repository Information](#repository-information)
[Key Components](#key-components)
[Dependencies](#dependencies)
Code Structure
The codebase is organized into two main components:
Python Server and Client Implementation - Core federated learning infrastructure
Mobile Application (Flutter/Dart) - Android client for real device participation
Python Implementation
The Python codebase implements a client-server federated learning architecture using:
FastAPI for RESTful server endpoints
PyTorch for neural network models and training
NumPy/Pandas for data processing
Rényi  Privacy (RDP) accountant for privacy budget tracking
Mobile Implementation
The mobile application is built using:
Flutter/Dart for cross-platform mobile development
Pure Dart implementation of Matrix Factorization model
HTTP client for server communication
Resource monitoring APIs for battery, CPU, and memory tracking
Folder Organization
Root Directory Structure
master_thesis/ ├── server.py                          # FastAPI server for federated learning ├── client.py                          # Python federated learning client ├── run_experiment.py                  # Single experiment runner ├── run_all_experiments.py             # Master experiment orchestrator ├── centralized_baseline.py            # Centralized training baseline ├── dp_sweep_experiment.py             #  privacy budget sweep ├── heterogeneity_sweep_experiment.py  # Data heterogeneity sweep ├── comprehensive_analysis.py          # Results analysis and visualization ├── analyze_results.py                 # Result analysis utilities ├── analyze_combined_results.py        # Combined results analysis ├── quick_summary.py                   # Quick result summaries ├── show_summary.py                    # Display experiment summaries ├── init_server_model.py               # Server model initialization ├── start_server.py                    # Server startup helper ├── test_data_collection.py            # Data collection testing ├── requirements.txt                   # Python dependencies ├── ratings.csv                        # MovieLens dataset │ ├── scripts/                           # Utility scripts │   ├── __init__.py │   ├── rdp_accountant.py             # RDP privacy accountant │   ├── metrics_collector.py          # Metrics collection utilities │   ├── recommendation_metrics.py      # Recommendation evaluation metrics │   └── attack_evaluation.py          # Privacy attack evaluation │ ├── federated_learning_in_mobile/      # Mobile application │   ├── lib/                           # Dart source code │   │   ├── main.dart                  # Main app entry point │   │   ├── models/ │   │   │   └── matrix_factorization.dart  # Matrix factorization model │   │   ├── services/ │   │   │   ├── api_client.dart       # HTTP client for server │   │   │   └── fl_client.dart        # Federated learning client logic │   │   └── utils/ │   │       ├── metrics_collector.dart    # Metrics collection │   │       ├── model_encoder.dart       # Model parameter encoding │   │       └── resource_monitor.dart     # Resource monitoring │   ├── android/                       # Android-specific configuration │   ├── ios/                           # iOS-specific configuration │   ├── pubspec.yaml                   # Flutter dependencies │   └── README.md                      # Mobile app documentation │ ├── results/                           # Experiment results (JSON/CSV) ├── mobile_results/                   # Mobile device experiment results ├── figures/                           # Generated plots and visualizations │   ├── accuracy_vs_epsilon.png │   ├── accuracy_vs_alpha.png │   ├── convergence.png │   ├── recommendation_metrics.png │   └── summary_table.csv │ └── [Documentation files]             # Various .md guide files
Key Directories Explained
scripts/ - Core Utilities
*rdp_accountant.py**: Implements the Rényi  Privacy accountant for tracking privacy budget consumption. Provides methods to compute RDP parameters, convert to (ε, δ)-DP, and track cumulative privacy loss across federated rounds.
*metrics_collector.py**: Handles collection and serialization of experiment metrics, including training loss, recommendation metrics, and resource usage statistics.
*recommendation_metrics.py**: Implements evaluation metrics for recommendation systems, including Hit Rate@K, NDCG@K, Precision@K, Recall@K, and F1-score.
*attack_evaluation.py**: Provides utilities for evaluating privacy attacks (membership inference, attribute inference, gradient inversion).
federated_learning_in_mobile/ - Mobile Application
*lib/main.dart**: Main Flutter application entry point, containing the UI for connecting to the server, running training rounds, and displaying metrics.
*lib/models/matrix_factorization.dart**: Pure Dart implementation of the Matrix Factorization model, compatible with the Python PyTorch version.
*lib/services/fl_client.dart**: Core federated learning client logic, handling model download, local training, and parameter upload.
*lib/services/api_client.dart**: HTTP client wrapper for communicating with the FastAPI server.
*lib/utils/**: Utility modules for model encoding/decoding, metrics collection, and resource monitoring.
results/ - Experiment Outputs
Contains JSON and CSV files with experiment results, including:
Per-round metrics (loss, accuracy, recommendation metrics)
Final aggregated results
Client-level statistics
Privacy budget consumption
figures/ - Visualizations
Generated plots and tables from comprehensive_analysis.py, including:
Privacy-utility trade-off curves
Convergence plots
Heterogeneity impact analysis
Summary statistics tables
Running the Code
Prerequisites
Python Environment (Python 3.8+)
   ```bash
   pip install -r requirements.txt
   ```
Dataset: Ensure ratings.csv (MovieLens 100K) is in the project root
Flutter SDK (for mobile app, optional): Flutter 3.9.2+
   ```bash
   cd federated_learning_in_mobile
   flutter pub get
   ```
Quick Start: Running All Experiments
The simplest way to run all experiments is using the master orchestrator:
```bash
# Terminal 1: Start the federated learning server
python server.py
# Terminal 2: Run all experiments
python run_all_experiments.py
```
This will execute:
Centralized baseline (no server needed)
DP sweep experiments (ε ∈ {∞, 8, 4, 2, 1})
Heterogeneity sweep experiments (α ∈ {0.1, 0.5, 1.0})
Comprehensive analysis and visualization
Estimated Time: 4-8 hours (depending on hardware)
Running Individual Experiments
1. Centralized Baseline
Establishes the performance upper bound without federated learning:
```bash
python centralized_baseline.py
```
Output: results/centralized_baseline.json
2.  Privacy Sweep
Evaluates accuracy-privacy trade-offs across different privacy budgets:
```bash
# Ensure server is running first
python server.py  # In another terminal
# Run DP sweep
python dp_sweep_experiment.py
```
Configuration: Tests ε ∈ {∞, 8, 4, 2, 1} with 3 random seeds each
Output: results/dp_*_alpha_0.5_dim_16_clients_100_seed_*.json
3. Heterogeneity Sweep
Assesses impact of non-IID data distributions:
```bash
# Server should still be running
python heterogeneity_sweep_experiment.py
```
Configuration: Tests α ∈ {0.1, 0.5, 1.0} with 3 random seeds each
Output: results/dp_*_alpha_*_dim_16_clients_100_seed_*.json
4. Single Experiment
Run a single federated learning experiment:
```bash
# Terminal 1: Start server
python server.py
# Terminal 2: Run experiment
python run_experiment.py
```
5. Analysis and Visualization
Generate plots and summary tables:
```bash
python comprehensive_analysis.py
```
Output: 
figures/accuracy_vs_epsilon.png
figures/accuracy_vs_alpha.png
figures/convergence.png
figures/recommendation_metrics.png
figures/summary_table.csv
Running with Mobile Devices
Server Setup
Start the server:
   ```bash
   python server.py
   ```
Make server accessible:
Local Network: Use your PC's local IP (e.g., 192.168.1.100:8000)
External Access: Use ngrok or similar tunneling service
Mobile App Setup
Navigate to mobile app directory:
   ```bash
   cd federated_learning_in_mobile
   ```
Install dependencies:
   ```bash
   flutter pub get
   ```
Run on Android device/emulator:
   ```bash
   flutter run
   ```
In the app:
Enter server URL (e.g., http://192.168.1.100:8000)
Enter unique Client ID
Click "Connect to Server"
Run training rounds
Hybrid Experiments
You can run experiments with both Python clients and mobile devices simultaneously:
Multiple Python clients (for large-scale parameter sweeps)
2+ Android devices (for real mobile validation)
All connect to the same server
Server aggregates all updates together
Repository Information
Code Repository
The complete codebase is available at:
https://github.com/jonathanjohn47/master_thesis.git
Repository Structure
The repository follows a standard Python/Flutter project structure with:
Clear separation between server, client, and mobile components
Modular scripts for different experiment types
Comprehensive documentation in markdown files
Results and figures directories for outputs
Version Control
The codebase uses Git for version control. Key branches:
main: Stable production code
experiments: Experimental features and variations
mobile: Mobile-specific implementations
Key Components
Server server.py)
The FastAPI server orchestrates federated learning:
Key Features:
RESTful API endpoints for client registration, model download, and parameter upload
Weighted federated averaging (FedAvg) aggregation
Model parameter serialization in portable JSON format (base64-encoded Float32)
Support for multiple concurrent clients (Python and mobile)
Health check and status endpoints
Main Endpoints:
POST /register: Client registration
GET /global-params-json: Download global model parameters
POST /upload-params-json: Upload local model updates
GET /healthz: Health check
POST /mobile-results: Receive mobile device metrics
Model Architecture:
Matrix Factorization with embedding dimension 16
User and item embedding matrices
MSE loss for rating prediction
Client client.py)
Python federated learning client implementation:
Key Features:
Local model training on client-specific data partitions
DP-SGD support with gradient clipping and noise addition
Non-IID data partitioning via Dirichlet distribution
Automatic server connection and round participation
Metrics collection and logging
Configuration:
Embedding dimension: 16
Learning rate: 0.01
Batch size: 32
Local epochs: 1
Gradient clipping norm: 1.0
DP noise multiplier: calibrated via RDP accountant
RDP Accountant scripts/rdp_accountant.py)
Privacy budget tracking using Rényi  Privacy:
Key Features:
Computes RDP parameters for Gaussian mechanism
Converts RDP to (ε, δ)-DP guarantees
Tracks cumulative privacy loss across rounds
Supports privacy amplification via subsampling
Binary search for optimal noise multiplier calibration
Mathematical Foundation:
RDP order α ∈ [1.1, 64]
Gaussian mechanism: RDP_α = α / (2σ²)
Composition: additive over rounds
Conversion: ε = min_α [RDP_α + log(1/δ)/(α-1)]
Mobile Client federated_learning_in_mobile/lib/)
Flutter/Dart mobile application:
Key Features:
Pure Dart Matrix Factorization model (compatible with Python version)
HTTP-based communication with FastAPI server
Resource monitoring (battery, CPU, memory)
Real-time metrics display
Results export to CSV/JSON
Architecture:
main.dart: UI and app orchestration
fl_client.dart: Federated learning client logic
api_client.dart: Server communication
matrix_factorization.dart: Model implementation
resource_monitor.dart: Device resource tracking

Code Execution Flow
Federated Learning Round
Server Initialization: Server initializes global model with random weights
Client Registration: Clients register with server and receive client IDs
Model Download: Clients download current global model parameters
Local Training: Each client trains locally on their data partition
Forward pass: compute predictions
Backward pass: compute gradients
DP-SGD: clip gradients and add noise (if enabled)
Update: apply optimizer step
Parameter Upload: Clients upload updated parameters to server
Aggregation: Server performs weighted FedAvg aggregation
Broadcast: Server broadcasts updated global model
Repeat: Steps 3-7 for multiple rounds
Experiment Execution
Data Loading: Load and preprocess MovieLens dataset
Data Partitioning: Split data into train/test sets and client partitions
Model Initialization: Initialize server model
Federated Training: Execute multiple federated rounds
Evaluation: Evaluate model on test set after each round
Metrics Collection: Collect and log all metrics
Result Serialization: Save results to JSON/CSV files
Analysis: Generate plots and summary tables
Troubleshooting
Common Issues
Server Connection Failed:
Ensure server is running: python server.py
Check server URL (use IP address, not localhost for mobile)
Verify network connectivity
Import Errors:
Install dependencies: pip install -r requirements.txt
Check Python version (3.8+)
Model Dimension Mismatch:
Ensure embedding dimensions match between server and clients
Check model initialization parameters
Mobile App Build Errors:
Run flutter pub get to install dependencies
Check Flutter SDK version: flutter --version
Verify Android SDK is properly configured
